Classification of Singular Terms: Ism, Kalima, and Ada
Author defines singular term and distinguishes noun, verb, and particle by their propositional role.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any singular term as a noun, verb, or particle based on its propositional role and time condition, and verify the definition of a noun.

Core Idea
A singular term is a noun if it can form a proposition without needing a source and does not carry external time.

In this lesson, we learn how logicians divide singular terms into three categories based on their function in a declarative proposition (qadiyya khabariyya). Understanding this division is essential for constructing proper definitions and arguments in the Islamic sciences, particularly in grammar and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The definiendum is the singular term (lafz mufrad). The author first divides it into two: those that can independently form a part of a declarative proposition and those that cannot.
2
Apply the first test: can it form a proposition part?
Check if the word can serve as subject or predicate in a declarative proposition. If yes, proceed; if no, it is a particle (ada).
Particles like 'fi' (in) cannot stand alone as subject or predicate.
3
Apply the second test: does it need a source and carry external time?
If the word can form a proposition without needing a source (masdar) to attribute meaning, and time external to its essence is not necessarily bound to its structure, then it is a noun (ism). Otherwise, it is a verb (kalima).
The verb 'yaktubu' needs a source (kitaba) and implies time.
4
Address the time objection
Some words like 'ams' (yesterday) seem to imply time, but the author clarifies that time is part of their meaning, not an external necessity. The noun's distinction is the absence of necessary external time, not of time altogether.
Words like 'zaman' (time) contain time in their signification, so they are still nouns.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللفظ المفرد إما أن يدل على ما يصح استقلاله بجعله أحد جزأي القضية الخبرية أو ليس فإن كان الأول فهو إما أن يصح تركب القضية منه ولا تفتقر إلى مصدر يسند إليه ولا يلزمه لبنيته زمان خارج عن هويته لزوماً ذهنياً أو ليس فإن كان الأول فهو الاسم، وإن كان الثاني فهو الكلمة وأمـا مـا هـو قسيم القسم الأول فهو الأداة، وعند هذا فيجب أن يعرف كل واحد منهـا علـى التفصيل وتقديم الاسم والكلمة على الأداة وإن كان واجباً نظراً إلى ما أشرنا إليه من الصفة الكمالية لكل واحد منهما، فتقديم الاسم على الكلمة أيضاً واجب بالنظر إلى هذا المعنى، إذ هو أشرف وأسمى، ولعل منشأ تسميته بالاسم إنما وقع مأخوذاً من هاهنا. والتحقيق في تميزه: إنما هو بأخذ ما أشرنا إليه من الخواص اللازمة له أو أخذ بعضها وذلك بأن يضاف إليه مع ما له أنه غير مفتقر إلى مصدر يستند إليه، ولا يلزمه لبنيته زمان خارج عن هويته، وأنه مما يصح تركب القضية الخبرية من حديه، وإن شئت اقتصرت في التعريف على واحدة منها، فإنه كافٍ على ما سنبينه، وهو كما في زيد) وعمرو) ونحوه، فإنه لا محالة غير مفتقر إلى مصدر يستند إليه وإن تصريفه ممتنع والقضية منتظمة من حديه كما في قولنا زيد (قائم) و(عمرو قاعد) ونحوه، وما يتخيل مـن ملازمة الزمان لمدلوله كما في أمس وغدا والزمان والصَّبوح والغبوق فالزمان داخل في مدلول لفظه وجزء من معناه لا أنه خارج لازم، وما هو خاصة الاسم إنما هو انتفاء ملازمة الزمان الخارج لمدلوله، وهي غير منتفية عنه في هذه الصور، وما يتخيل من ملازمة الزمان الخارجي لمدلولات بعض الأسماء كـ زيد) وعمرو من ضرورة وجودها فيه فليس ذلك مما هو لازم لبنيته
The singular utterance either indicates something whose independence is sound by making it one of the two parts of a declarative proposition, or not. If the first, then either the proposition can be composed from it without needing a source (masdar) to be attributed to it, and time external to its essence is not necessarily bound to its structure mentally, or not. If the first, then it is the noun (ism). If the second, then it is the verb (kalima). As for what is the counterpart of the first division, it is the particle (ada). At this point, each of them must be known in detail, and the noun and verb are given precedence over the particle, although it is obligatory, in view of what we indicated regarding the perfection attribute of each of them. So giving precedence to the noun over the verb is also obligatory in view of this meaning, since it is nobler and higher. Perhaps the origin of its name 'ism' is taken from here. The verification of its distinction is only by taking what we indicated of the necessary properties for it, or taking some of them, namely that it is added to what it has that it does not need a source to be attributed to, and that time external to its essence is not necessarily bound to its structure, and that it is such that the declarative proposition can be composed from its two limits (hadd). If you wish, you may restrict the definition to one of them, for it is sufficient, as we will explain. And it is like in Zayd and Amr and the like, for it is undoubtedly not in need of a source to be attributed to, its inflection is impossible, and the proposition is ordered from its two limits as in our saying 'Zayd is standing' and 'Amr is sitting' and the like. And what is imagined of the necessity of time for its meaning, like yesterday, tomorrow, time, morning-drink, and evening-drink, so time is inside the meaning of its utterance and part of its signification, not externally necessary. And what is specific to the noun is only the absence of the necessity of external time for its meaning, and it is not absent in these forms. And what is imagined of the necessity of external time for the meanings of some nouns like Zayd and Amr due to the necessity of their existence in it, that is not something that is necessary to its structure.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage defines the division of singular terms into noun, verb, and particle based on their role in a declarative proposition. The author begins by stating that a singular term (lafz mufrad) either can independently serve as one of the two parts of a declarative proposition (qadiyya khabariyya) or not. If it can, then it is either such that the proposition can be composed from it without needing a source (masdar) to be attributed to it, and time external to its essence is not necessarily associated with its structure mentally. If these conditions hold, it is a noun. If not, it is a verb. The counterpart of the first division (i.e., terms that cannot independently form a proposition part) is the particle. The author then explains the order of priority: noun and verb are given precedence over the particle due to their perfection, and the noun is given precedence over the verb because it is nobler. The verification of the noun's distinctness is through its properties: it does not need a source, it does not necessarily involve external time, and it can form the two limits of a declarative proposition. Examples: Zayd and Amr can be subjects. The author addresses a potential objection: words like 'yesterday' or 'time' seem to imply time, but time is part of their meaning, not externally necessary. For words like Zayd, the external time is due to existence, not the structure of the word itself. Thus, the defining characteristic of the noun is the absence of necessary external time.

Key Terms
اللفظ المفرد
الاسم
الكلمة
الأداة
القضية الخبرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a characteristic that distinguishes a noun (ism) from a verb (kalima)?
A) It does not need a source (masdar) to be attributed to
B) Time external to its essence is not necessarily bound to its structure
C) It can form the two limits of a declarative proposition
D) It is always indefinite in meaning

Key Takeaway
The noun is distinguished by its ability to form a proposition without needing a source and without necessarily implying external time, setting it apart from verbs and particles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following Arabic words as ism, kalima, or ada based on the criteria from the passage: 'kitab' (book), 'yaktubu' (he writes), 'fi' (in), 'qamar' (moon), 'shams' (sun). For each, justify your classification by referencing the definitional properties.
?
Explain why 'ams' (yesterday) is considered an ism despite referring to time, using the author's distinction between time as part of meaning versus external necessity.

Defining Universal and Particular Terms
Author defines universal and particular using capacity for sharing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given single word as universal or particular based on the criterion of conceptual capacity for sharing.

Core Idea
Universality is about the capacity to be shared, not about actual occurrence.

In Islamic logic, the first step in analyzing terms is to determine whether a word is universal (كلي) or particular (جزئي). This lesson unpacks the author’s definition and the crucial role of ‘capacity for sharing’ (صلاحية للشركة).

Guided Path
1
Understand the criterion
The author states: a single word is universal if its concept can be shared by many; otherwise it is particular. Note that universality is not intrinsic but depends on the subject.
2
Distinguish between actual sharing and capacity
The author emphasizes that actual occurrence of sharing is irrelevant. Even if a term has only one instance, it may still be universal if its concept could apply to more than one.
3
Apply to examples
Consider 'human' (actual sharing), 'mountain of gold' (potential sharing), and 'sun' (conceptual capacity despite external uniqueness).
The impossibility of multiple suns is due to external factors, not the concept itself.
4
Identify particular terms
A term like 'this sun' is particular because it points to a specific individual and cannot be shared.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس في قسمة المفرد إلى الكلي والجزئي. نقول: اللفظ المفرد إما أن يكون لمفهومه صلاحية أن يشترك فيه كثيرون أو ليس، فإن كان الأول فـ (كلي)، وإن كان الثاني فـ (جزئي). وليست كلية اللفظ وجزئيته لذاته، بل كليّته وجزئيته لكليّة موضوعه وجزئيته. ولما كان تعريف الجزئي إنما هو بسلب ما وجب للكلي، والإيجاب أشرف من السلب وأبسط من جهة أن السلب إنما هو سلب لنسبة إيجاب، وليس الإيجاب إيجاباً لنسبة سلب، فالسلب لا يتم دون الإيجاب، والإيجاب يتم دونه. وإن ما يحصل للنفس الناطقة من الكمال بالجزئي أدنى مما يحصل لها من الكلي، وأنه محسوس فلا يطلب في العلوم النظرية وجب تقديم النظر في الكلي أولاً. فنقول: قد ثبت أن معنى الكلي هو ما مفهومه يصلح لاشتراك كثيرين فيه. وما يصلح لذلك فقد يتفق وقوع الاشتراك فيه بالفعل كاسم الإنسان والفرس ونحوه، وقد يتفق عدم وقوع الاشتراك فيه لكن منه ما لو وقع لما كان ممتنعاً كـ (جبل من ذهب) و (بحر من زئبق) ونحوه، ومنه ما لو قدّر وقوعه لزم عنه المحال كاسم الشمس والقمر ونحوه. لكن ما مثل هذا الامتناع إنما هو لأمر خارج عن مفهوم اسم الشمس والقمر لا لنفس مفهوميها. وعدم وقوع الاشتراك في مفهوم الاسم بالفعل أو لتوهم إذا كان على سبيل الاتفاق كما ذكرناه من المثال أو بسبب خارج عن نفس المفهوم كما في الشمس والقمر، فإنه لا يمنع من الصلاحية للاشتراك فلا يمنع من الكليّة، إذ كليّة اللفظ إنما هو بسبب صلاحية مفهومه للشركة لا لنفس وقوع الشركة بالفعل، أو التوهم كما ظن قوم. فعلى هذا لفظ الشمس والقمر كلي؛ لأنه صالح للشركة لا هذه الشمس وهذا القمر. وكذا كل ما يقع في امتداد الإشارة إليه إذ هو غير صالح للشركة، فإنه لا يتصور أن تكون هذه الشمس مشتركة بين.
Chapter Five: On the division of the single word into universal and particular. We say: the single word either has a concept that can be shared by many or not. If the first, it is universal; if the second, it is particular. The universality and particularity of a word are not intrinsic to it, but rather due to the universality and particularity of its subject. Since the definition of the particular is only by negating what is necessary for the universal, and affirmation is nobler than negation and simpler because negation is only the negation of an affirmative relation, and affirmation is not affirmation of a negative relation, negation is not complete without affirmation, while affirmation is complete without it. Since the perfection attained by the rational soul from the particular is lower than that from the universal, and since it is sensible and not sought in theoretical sciences, it is necessary to consider the universal first. So we say: it is established that the meaning of universal is that whose concept can be shared by many. That which can be so shared may actually have sharing occur, like the names 'human' and 'horse', etc., or may not actually have sharing. Among those that do not actually have sharing, some would not be impossible if they did occur, like 'a mountain of gold' and 'a sea of mercury', etc. And some, if they were imagined to occur, would entail impossibility, like the names 'sun' and 'moon', etc. But the impossibility in such cases is due to something external to the concept of the names 'sun' and 'moon', not to their concepts themselves. The non-occurrence of sharing in the concept of a name, whether actual or due to some assumption if it is accidental as in our example, or due to something external to the concept itself as in 'sun' and 'moon', does not prevent the capacity for sharing, and thus does not prevent universality. For the universality of a word is only due to the capacity of its concept for sharing, not due to the actual occurrence of sharing or assumption as some people thought. Thus the words 'sun' and 'moon' are universal because they are capable of sharing, unlike 'this sun' and 'this moon'. And similarly everything that falls under the extension of pointing to it is not capable of sharing, for it is inconceivable that 'this sun' be shared among many.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage defines the fundamental division of single words into universal (kullī) and particular (juz'ī). The author begins by stating the criterion: a word is universal if its concept can apply to many individuals; otherwise it is particular. Importantly, universality is not intrinsic to the word itself but depends on the subject it signifies. The author then digresses on the nobility of affirmation over negation, explaining that since the particular is defined by negation of the universal, and affirmation is prior, the universal should be studied first. The core point then returns: the meaning of 'universal' is capacity for sharing (ṣalāḥiyya li-l-shirka). Actual sharing is not required. For example, 'human' and 'horse' actually apply to many, but 'mountain of gold' could apply to many if such mountains existed—it's not logically impossible. However, 'sun' and 'moon' appear to be unique, but the impossibility of many suns is due to external factors (the physical universe), not the concepts themselves. Thus 'sun' is still a universal word because its concept could, in principle, apply to more than one. The author rejects the view of those who demand actual or assumed sharing. The particular, like 'this sun', is not universal because it is tied to a specific individual and cannot be shared.

Key Terms
كلي
جزئي
صلاحية للشركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the word 'sun' (shams) to be universal?
A) Because the sun has many rays.
B) Because its concept could apply to more than one sun if the universe allowed.
C) Because it is a common noun in Arabic.
D) Because it is not a proper name.

Key Takeaway
A term's universality depends on the capacity of its concept for sharing, not on actual occurrence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following terms as universal or particular, and explain your reasoning using the author's criterion: 'this prayer', 'prayer', 'the Kaaba', 'a mosque'.
?
Find a term in a fiqh text (e.g., 'salah', 'zakat') and determine whether it is universal or particular. Provide evidence from its usage.

The Capacity vs Actual Sharing: Clarifying a Common Misunderstanding
Author explains that universality is based on potential sharing, not actual occurrence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between capacity for sharing and actual sharing, and evaluate whether a term is universal even if it has only one instance in reality.

Core Idea
A term remains universal even if it is instantiated only once, as long as its concept could apply to more than one.

A common mistake is to think that words like 'sun' are particular because there is only one sun. The author explicitly addresses this and explains that the impossibility of multiple suns is due to external factors, not the concept. This lesson focuses on that nuance and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim
The author states that universality is about صلاحية (capacity), not وقوع (actual occurrence). This is the key to understanding the classification.
2
Examine the three categories of terms
The author lists terms with actual sharing (human, horse), terms with potential but no actual sharing (mountain of gold), and terms with external impossibility (sun, moon). All are universal because they have capacity.
3
Understand external vs internal impossibility
For 'sun', the impossibility of multiple instances comes from the physical universe, not from the concept itself. The concept of 'sun' does not entail uniqueness.
4
Apply to new examples
Consider 'the Prophet Muhammad' – this is particular because it refers to a specific individual. But 'prophet' is universal. The same logic applies to 'the Kaaba' versus 'mosque'.

الفصل الخامس في قسمة المفرد إلى الكلي والجزئي. نقول: اللفظ المفرد إما أن يكون لمفهومه صلاحية أن يشترك فيه كثيرون أو ليس، فإن كان الأول فـ (كلي)، وإن كان الثاني فـ (جزئي). وليست كلية اللفظ وجزئيته لذاته، بل كليّته وجزئيته لكليّة موضوعه وجزئيته. ولما كان تعريف الجزئي إنما هو بسلب ما وجب للكلي، والإيجاب أشرف من السلب وأبسط من جهة أن السلب إنما هو سلب لنسبة إيجاب، وليس الإيجاب إيجاباً لنسبة سلب، فالسلب لا يتم دون الإيجاب، والإيجاب يتم دونه. وإن ما يحصل للنفس الناطقة من الكمال بالجزئي أدنى مما يحصل لها من الكلي، وأنه محسوس فلا يطلب في العلوم النظرية وجب تقديم النظر في الكلي أولاً. فنقول: قد ثبت أن معنى الكلي هو ما مفهومه يصلح لاشتراك كثيرين فيه. وما يصلح لذلك فقد يتفق وقوع الاشتراك فيه بالفعل كاسم الإنسان والفرس ونحوه، وقد يتفق عدم وقوع الاشتراك فيه لكن منه ما لو وقع لما كان ممتنعاً كـ (جبل من ذهب) و (بحر من زئبق) ونحوه، ومنه ما لو قدّر وقوعه لزم عنه المحال كاسم الشمس والقمر ونحوه. لكن ما مثل هذا الامتناع إنما هو لأمر خارج عن مفهوم اسم الشمس والقمر لا لنفس مفهوميها. وعدم وقوع الاشتراك في مفهوم الاسم بالفعل أو لتوهم إذا كان على سبيل الاتفاق كما ذكرناه من المثال أو بسبب خارج عن نفس المفهوم كما في الشمس والقمر، فإنه لا يمنع من الصلاحية للاشتراك فلا يمنع من الكليّة، إذ كليّة اللفظ إنما هو بسبب صلاحية مفهومه للشركة لا لنفس وقوع الشركة بالفعل، أو التوهم كما ظن قوم. فعلى هذا لفظ الشمس والقمر كلي؛ لأنه صالح للشركة لا هذه الشمس وهذا القمر. وكذا كل ما يقع في امتداد الإشارة إليه إذ هو غير صالح للشركة، فإنه لا يتصور أن تكون هذه الشمس مشتركة بين.
Chapter Five: On the division of the single word into universal and particular. We say: the single word either has a concept that can be shared by many or not. If the first, it is universal; if the second, it is particular. The universality and particularity of a word are not intrinsic to it, but rather due to the universality and particularity of its subject. Since the definition of the particular is only by negating what is necessary for the universal, and affirmation is nobler than negation and simpler because negation is only the negation of an affirmative relation, and affirmation is not affirmation of a negative relation, negation is not complete without affirmation, while affirmation is complete without it. Since the perfection attained by the rational soul from the particular is lower than that from the universal, and since it is sensible and not sought in theoretical sciences, it is necessary to consider the universal first. So we say: it is established that the meaning of universal is that whose concept can be shared by many. That which can be so shared may actually have sharing occur, like the names 'human' and 'horse', etc., or may not actually have sharing. Among those that do not actually have sharing, some would not be impossible if they did occur, like 'a mountain of gold' and 'a sea of mercury', etc. And some, if they were imagined to occur, would entail impossibility, like the names 'sun' and 'moon', etc. But the impossibility in such cases is due to something external to the concept of the names 'sun' and 'moon', not to their concepts themselves. The non-occurrence of sharing in the concept of a name, whether actual or due to some assumption if it is accidental as in our example, or due to something external to the concept itself as in 'sun' and 'moon', does not prevent the capacity for sharing, and thus does not prevent universality. For the universality of a word is only due to the capacity of its concept for sharing, not due to the actual occurrence of sharing or assumption as some people thought. Thus the words 'sun' and 'moon' are universal because they are capable of sharing, unlike 'this sun' and 'this moon'. And similarly everything that falls under the extension of pointing to it is not capable of sharing, for it is inconceivable that 'this sun' be shared among many.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage defines the fundamental division of single words into universal (kullī) and particular (juz'ī). The author begins by stating the criterion: a word is universal if its concept can apply to many individuals; otherwise it is particular. Importantly, universality is not intrinsic to the word itself but depends on the subject it signifies. The author then digresses on the nobility of affirmation over negation, explaining that since the particular is defined by negation of the universal, and affirmation is prior, the universal should be studied first. The core point then returns: the meaning of 'universal' is capacity for sharing (ṣalāḥiyya li-l-shirka). Actual sharing is not required. For example, 'human' and 'horse' actually apply to many, but 'mountain of gold' could apply to many if such mountains existed—it's not logically impossible. However, 'sun' and 'moon' appear to be unique, but the impossibility of many suns is due to external factors (the physical universe), not the concepts themselves. Thus 'sun' is still a universal word because its concept could, in principle, apply to more than one. The author rejects the view of those who demand actual or assumed sharing. The particular, like 'this sun', is not universal because it is tied to a specific individual and cannot be shared.

According to the author, why is the term 'mountain of gold' considered universal even though no such mountain actually exists?

C) Because it is not a proper name.
D) Because it is physically possible to have multiple such mountains.

Key Takeaway
Do not confuse uniqueness in reality with particularity in logic; a term is universal if its concept can apply to more than one, even if it actually does not.

The Five Universals
beginner
5 min
Defining Universal and Particular
Author defines kulli and juz'i and establishes logical priority of kulli.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a universal term from a particular term based on the suitability of its concept for participation by many.

Core Idea
Universality is about conceptual suitability, not how many individuals actually exist.

When we encounter a term in Islamic sciences, we must know whether its meaning can apply to multiple individuals or only one. The author of this passage sets the foundation by dividing single expressions into universal and particular based on whether their concepts are suitable for sharing. This distinction is crucial for definitions, syllogisms, and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the division criterion
The author divides single expressions (al-lafz al-mufrad) into two types based on whether their concept (mafhum) has the suitability (salahiyya) for many to share in it. If yes, it is universal (kulli); if not, it is particular (juz'i). This is the primary logical division.
2
Notice the definition of particular
The particular is defined negatively: it is what is not universal. The author notes that negation depends on affirmation, so the universal is more fundamental and therefore studied first.
This reflects the logical priority of the universal in theoretical sciences.
3
Extract the core definition
The author confirms: the meaning of 'universal' is that its concept is suitable for the participation of many. This is the operational definition you will apply.
Memorize: kulli = mafhumuhu yaslahu li-ishtirak kathirin fihi.
4
Distinguish from actual sharing
The author emphasizes that suitability (salahiyya) is not the same as actual occurrence (wujud bi al-fi'l). A term can be universal even if it has only one instance, as long as its meaning could be shared.
This refutes a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقول: اللفظ المفرد إما أن يكون لمفهومه صلاحية أن يشترك فيه كثيرون أو ليس، فإن كان الأول فـ (كلي)، وإن كان الثاني فـ (جزئي). وليست كلية اللفظ وجزئيته لذاته، بل كليّته وجزئيته لكليّة موضوعه وجزئيته ولما كان تعريف الجزئي إنما هو بسلب ما وجب للكلي، والإيجاب أشرف من السلب وأبسط من جهة أن السلب إنما هو سلب لنسبة إيجاب، وليس الإيجاب إيجاباً لنسبة سلب فالسلب لا يتم دون الإيجاب، والإيجاب يتم دونه. وإن ما يحصل للنفس الناطقة من الكمال بالجزئي أدنى مما يحصل لها من الكلي، وأنه محسوس فلا يطلب في العلوم النظرية وجب تقديم النظر في الكلي أولاً. فنقول : قد ثبت أن معنى الكلي هو ما مفهومه يصلح لاشتراك كثيرين فيه، وما يصلح لذلك فقد يتفق وقوع الاشتراك فيه بالفعل كاسم الإنسان والفرس ونحوه، وقد يتفق عدم وقوع الاشتراك فيه لكن منه ما لو وقع لما كان ممتنعاً كـ (جبل من ذهب) و (بحر من زئبق) ونحوه، ومنه ما لو قدّر وقوعه لزم عنه المحال كاسم الشمس والقمر ونحوه، لكن ما مثل هذا الامتناع إنما هو لأمر خارج عن مفهوم اسم الشمس والقمر لا لنفس مفهوميها، وعدم وقوع الاشتراك في مفهوم الاسم بالفعل أو لتوهم إذا كان على سبيل الاتفاق كما ذكرناه من المثال أو بسبب خارج عن نفس المفهوم كما في الشمس والقمر ، فإنه لا يمنع من الصلاحية للاشتراك فلا يمنع من الكليّة، إذ كليّة اللفظ إنما هو بسبب صلاحية مفهومه للشركة لا لنفس وقوع الشركة بالفعل، أو التوهم كما ظن قوم، فعلى هذا لفظ الشمس والقمر كلي؛ لأنه صالح للشركة لا هذه الشمس وهذا القمر، وكذا كل ما يقع في امتداد الإشارة إليه إذ هو غير صالح للشركة، فإنه لا يتصور أن تكون هذه الشمس مشتركة بين شموس كثيرة إلا أن يؤخذ معناها وبقطع النظر عن خصوصياتها، فيكون المعنى إذ ذاك كلياً
We say: The single expression is either such that its concept has the suitability that many participate in it, or not. If the first, then it is 'universal'; if the second, then it is 'particular'. And the universality and particularity of the expression are not intrinsic to it, but rather its universality and particularity are due to the universality or particularity of its subject. Since the definition of the particular is only by negating what is necessary for the universal, and affirmation is nobler than negation and simpler in that negation is only a negation of an affirmative relation, and affirmation is not an affirmation of a negative relation, so negation is not complete without affirmation, while affirmation is complete without it. And the perfection attained by the rational soul through the particular is lower than that attained through the universal, and it is sensible, so it is not sought in theoretical sciences. Therefore it is necessary to give precedence to the consideration of the universal first. So we say: It has been established that the meaning of 'universal' is that whose concept is suitable for the participation of many in it. And what is suitable for that may happen to actually have participation in it, like the name 'human' and 'horse' and the like. And it may happen that participation does not occur, but among these there is what, if it occurred, would not be impossible, like 'a mountain of gold' and 'a sea of mercury' and the like. And among them there is what, if its occurrence were supposed, would entail impossibility, like the name 'sun' and 'moon' and the like. But this impossibility is only due to something external to the concept of the name 'sun' and 'moon', not due to the concepts themselves. And the non-occurrence of participation in the concept of the name actually, or due to a supposition if it is by way of coincidence as we mentioned in the example, or due to something external to the concept itself as in the case of sun and moon, does not prevent suitability for participation, so it does not prevent universality. For the universality of an expression is only due to the suitability of its concept for sharing, not due to the actual occurrence of sharing or its supposition, as some people thought. Thus the word 'sun' and 'moon' are universal because they are suitable for sharing, not this particular sun and this particular moon. And likewise everything that falls under the extension of a pointer is not suitable for sharing, for it is inconceivable that this particular sun is shared among many suns unless its meaning is taken, disregarding its particularities, so the meaning then is universal.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage defines the universal (kulli) and the particular (juz'i) in the context of logical terms. The author begins by stating the division of the single term (al-lafz al-mufrad) into universal and particular based on the suitability of its concept for participation by many. If the concept can be shared by many individuals, it is called 'universal'; if not, it is 'particular'. The author then clarifies that universality and particularity are not intrinsic to the word itself but derive from the universality or particularity of its referent or subject matter. He explains that the definition of the particular involves negation of what is necessary for the universal, and notes that affirmation is more fundamental than negation. Because the particular is sensible and yields lower perfection, theoretical sciences prioritize the universal. The core of the passage is the confirmation that the meaning of 'universal' is the suitability for participation. The author distinguishes cases: some universals actually have multiple instances (e.g., 'human'), some could have but do not (e.g., 'mountain of gold' is physically impossible but conceptually suitable), and some entail impossibility if multiple instances were supposed (e.g., 'sun' as a unique celestial body). However, the impossibility in the latter case is due to external factors (e.g., astronomical uniqueness), not the concept itself. Thus, universality is based on conceptual suitability, not actual or possible instantiation. Therefore, terms like 'sun' are still universal because the meaning can be shared, though no particular sun can be shared. This refutes those who mistakenly think actual sharing is required for universality.

D) It must be used in theoretical sciences only.

Key Takeaway
A universal (kulli) is defined by the suitability of its concept for sharing; actual or possible multiplicity is not required.

The Five Universals

5 min
Suitability vs. Actual Sharing
Author clarifies that universality is based on suitability, not actual or possible instantiation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify terms as universal even when they have only one instance, by distinguishing conceptual suitability from actual sharing.

Core Idea
A term is universal if its meaning can be shared, even if it never is.

Many students mistakenly think that a universal term must have multiple actual examples. This passage corrects that error by analyzing examples like 'mountain of gold' and 'sun'. The key is that universality depends on whether the concept itself allows sharing, not on whether sharing actually occurs or is even possible in the physical world.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of universal
A universal is a term whose concept is suitable (salih) for participation by many. This is the fixed criterion.
Internalize this definition before proceeding.
2
Categorize the examples
The author gives three types: (1) actual sharing (human, horse), (2) no sharing but possible (mountain of gold), (3) no sharing and impossible due to external reasons (sun, moon). All are universal.
The impossibility in case 3 is not from the concept itself.
3
Identify the common misunderstanding refuted
Some people (qawm) think universality requires actual sharing or its supposition. The author rejects this explicitly.
The phrase 'as some people thought' indicates a refutation.
4
Apply the distinction
When you encounter a term that seems to refer to only one thing (e.g., 'the Kaaba'), ask: Does its concept allow multiple instances? If yes (the concept of a sacred building could apply to multiple), then the term is universal, even if only one exists.
This prevents confusion in logical analysis.

7 min
The Universal's Mental Existence: Proof from the Trilemma
Proving universals exist in the mind via a trilemma and refuting Stoics

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the trilemma argument that proves universals exist in the mind and refute the Stoic denial.

Core Idea
Universals exist mentally, not as fictions: the trilemma forces them to be real abstractions.

The Stoics claimed that universals are mere imaginary constructs. The author responds with a powerful logical argument: the individuals of a species must share some commonality, otherwise they couldn't all be called by the same name. This lesson walks you through that argument and shows why mental existence is the only viable option.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Stoic position
The Stoics (al-Kalbiyyun) claimed that the universal is a freely imagined thing (wahmiyyun mursal), not subsistent or existing.
2
Set up the trilemma
The author considers three possibilities for individuals of a species: identical in every respect, different in every respect, or identical in some respects and different in others.
3
Eliminate the first two possibilities
If identical in every respect, there would be no multiplicity, but individuals are multiple. If different in every respect, then one individual being human would prevent any other from being human, which is false.
4
Accept the third possibility
The only remaining option is that individuals agree in some respects and differ in others. The aspect of agreement can be conceived separately; that conceived meaning is the universal.
5
Conclude mental existence
The universal's existence is mental (wujud 'aqli), it is not a fiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى ما حققناه من معنى الكلي لا يمكن أن يكون موجوداً في الأشخاص وإلا كان ما هو صالح لوقوع الشركة فيه منحصراً فيها لا يقبل الشركة وهو محال. وليس كما ظن الكلبيين أن الكلي وهمي مرسل غير متقوم ولا موجود، فإنه وإن لم يكن له وجود في الأعيان ولا في الأشخاص فإنه موجود في العقل، فإن كل عاقل يجد من نفسه وفي نفسه وجود معان لو نسبها إلى المعاني الشخصية كانت مطابقة لمعانيها على نحو ما يتمثل من معنى الإنسان والفرس وغيره، كيف وإنَّا نعلم أن أشخاص نوع الإنسان مثلاً إما أن يتفق من كل وجه أو يفترق من كل وجه، أو تتفق من وجه وتفترق من وجه، فإن اتفقت من كل وجه فلا تَعدُّدَ وهي متعددة، وإن اختلفت من كل وجه فإن كان الواحد منها إنساناً وجب أن لا يكون شيء من باقي الأشخاص إنساناً وهو خلف، فلم يبق إلا أن تكون متفقة من وجه دون وجه وعند ذلك يخفى جواز قطع النظر عن ما به الافتراق مع تصور ما به الاتفاق وذلك المتصور هو المعنى بالكلي، وليس معنى كون الكلي موجوداً في العقل غير أن ماهيته متميزة في نفس العاقل لا بمعنى أن صورته قائمة بالعاقل حتى يقال إن مَنْ عقل الحرارة والبرودة تعقلاً كلياً يكون حاراً أو بارداً، أو إن كل من قامت به الحرارة والبرودة يكون عاقلاً للحرارة والبرودة كـ (الجمادات) وإذا تحقق معنى الكلي في الأذهان فمنه ما هو موجود بعد الكثرة كالحاصل في النفس من معنى الإنسان عند حصول صورة زيد وعمرو في الخيال، ومنه ما هو موجود قبل الكثرة وهو ما يكون حصول المعاني الشخصية المطابقة له الأعيان متأخرة عن وجوده في العقل كالحاصل بالنسبة إلى الأنفس الفلكية وهو ما يعبر عنه قوم بالمثل، بل وكما بالنسبة إلى الواحد منا فإنه قد يتصور في نفسه معنى، ثم يحصل بعد ذلك من الأشخاص ما نسبته إليها نسبة ما لو كان مأخوذاً منها كان ذلك منسوباً إلى فعله أو فعل غيره، وليس ذلك مما لا يكون إلا جسماً كما ظن الرواقيون - بناءاً على اعتقادهم أن كل كلي جسم - إلا أن يكون عنايتهم بلفظ الجسم الوجود، إذ
And according to what we have established of the meaning of the universal, it cannot exist in individuals, otherwise that which is suitable for the occurrence of participation in it would be restricted to them and would not accept participation, which is impossible. And it is not as the Kalbiyun (Stoics) thought that the universal is a freely imagined thing, not subsistent and not existing. For even though it has no existence in external entities nor in individuals, it exists in the mind. For every rational person finds in himself and in his own self the existence of meanings such that if he related them to the particular meanings, they would correspond to those meanings, in the manner of the representation of the meaning of 'human', 'horse', etc. How so? Indeed we know that the individuals of a species like 'human', for example, are either identical in every respect or differ in every respect, or agree in some respect and differ in some respect. If they were identical in every respect, there would be no multiplicity, while they are multiple. If they differed in every respect, then if one of them is a human, it would be necessary that none of the remaining individuals be a human, which is a contradiction. So no alternative remains except that they agree in some respect and not in another. Thereupon it is possible to disregard the aspect of difference while conceiving the aspect of agreement, and that conceived something is the universal meaning. The meaning of the universal's existence in the mind is nothing other than that its quiddity (mahiyya) is distinguished in the rational soul—not in the sense that its form inheres in the rational being such that one who conceives heat and cold in a universal way would become hot or cold, or that everything in which heat and cold inhere conceives heat and cold, like inanimate objects. And when the reality of the universal in minds is established, among it there is that which exists after multiplicity, like what obtains in the soul of the meaning of 'human' upon obtaining the images of Zayd and 'Amr in the imagination. And among it there is that which exists before multiplicity, which is when the obtaining of the particular meanings corresponding to it from external entities is posterior to its existence in the mind, like what obtains in relation to the celestial souls, which some call 'exemplars' (al-muthul). Similarly, in relation to one of us: he may conceive in himself a meaning, and thereafter individuals obtain whose relation to it is the relation of what, if it had been taken from them, would be attributed to his action or the action of another. And this is not something that cannot be except a body, as the Stoics thought, based on their belief that every universal is a body—unless their concern with the word 'body' is 'existence', since
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage defends the reality of universals in the mind and refutes the Stoic position that universals are mere mental fictions (wahmiyyun mursal). The author begins by asserting that the universal cannot exist in external individuals, because if it did, that which is suitable for participation would be confined to those individuals, making further participation impossible. This contradicts the definition of universal as suitable for sharing. The author then explicitly denies the Stoic view, stating that although the universal has no external existence, it does exist in the mind. Every rational person finds within themselves the presence of universal meanings that correspond to particular instances. To prove this, the author presents a logical argument by considering the individuals of a species: they must be either completely identical, completely different, or partly identical and partly different. Complete identity would negate multiplicity, which is false because individuals are many. Complete difference would mean that if one individual is, say, human, then none of the others could be human, which is contradictory. Therefore, they must agree in some respect and differ in another. From this, we can abstract the aspect of agreement, disregarding differences, and that abstracted meaning is the universal. The author clarifies that mental existence does not mean the universal's form inheres in the rational soul such that conceiving heat would make one hot; rather, it means the quiddity (mahiyya) is mentally distinguished. Once mental existence is established, the author distinguishes two types: (1) universal after multiplicity (kulli ba'd al-kathra), like the concept of 'human' derived from perceiving Zayd and 'Amr, and (2) universal before multiplicity (kulli qabl al-kathra), where the universal is conceived first and then particular instances come later, as in the case of celestial souls (al-nufus al-falakiyya) or even human inventors who conceive a design before making individual artifacts. The passage ends by criticizing the Stoics for materializing universals, suggesting they used 'body' to mean existence itself.

Universals have a real mental existence proven by a disjunctive argument; they are not mere fictions.

Two Modes of Universals: After and Before Multiplicity
Distinguishing universals after and before multiplicity, clarifying mental existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the universal after multiplicity (kulli ba'd al-kathra) and the universal before multiplicity (kulli qabl al-kathra) and provide examples from Islamic thought.

Core Idea
Universals can be abstracted from particulars or pre-exist as mental designs before creation.

Having established that universals exist in the mind, the author further divides them into two kinds: those that are derived from multiple instances after experience, and those that are conceived before the actual existence of their instances. This distinction is crucial for understanding how humans and celestial souls acquire knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of universal
The universal is a meaning that can be shared among many individuals.
2
Identify the first type: after multiplicity
The author says: 'among it there is that which exists after multiplicity, like what obtains in the soul of the meaning of 'human' upon obtaining images of Zayd and 'Amr.' This is the universal abstracted from particulars.
3
Identify the second type: before multiplicity
The author says: 'among it there is that which exists before multiplicity, which is when the obtaining of particular meanings is posterior to its existence in the mind.' Examples: in celestial souls, and in human inventors who conceive a design before making artifacts.
4
Compare the two
One is derived from individuals after experience, the other precedes them and is conceived independently.
5
Apply to Islamic examples
Think of a concept like 'riba' (usury) which is derived from specific transactions (after multiplicity) versus the concept of 'tawhid' which is intellectually prior (before multiplicity).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى ما حققناه من معنى الكلي لا يمكن أن يكون موجوداً في الأشخاص وإلا كان ما هو صالح لوقوع الشركة فيه منحصراً فيها لا يقبل الشركة وهو محال. وليس كما ظن الكلبيين أن الكلي وهمي مرسل غير متقوم ولا موجود، فإنه وإن لم يكن له وجود في الأعيان ولا في الأشخاص فإنه موجود في العقل، فإن كل عاقل يجد من نفسه وفي نفسه وجود معان لو نسبها إلى المعاني الشخصية كانت مطابقة لمعانيها على نحو ما يتمثل من معنى الإنسان والفرس وغيره، كيف وإنَّا نعلم أن أشخاص نوع الإنسان مثلاً إما أن يتفق من كل وجه أو يفترق من كل وجه، أو تتفق من وجه وتفترق من وجه، فإن اتفقت من كل وجه فلا تَعدُّدَ وهي متعددة، وإن اختلفت من كل وجه فإن كان الواحد منها إنساناً وجب أن لا يكون شيء من باقي الأشخاص إنساناً وهو خلف، فلم يبق إلا أن تكون متفقة من وجه دون وجه وعند ذلك يخفى جواز قطع النظر عن ما به الافتراق مع تصور ما به الاتفاق وذلك المتصور هو المعنى بالكلي، وليس معنى كون الكلي موجوداً في العقل غير أن ماهيته متميزة في نفس العاقل لا بمعنى أن صورته قائمة بالعاقل حتى يقال إن مَنْ عقل الحرارة والبرودة تعقلاً كلياً يكون حاراً أو بارداً، أو إن كل من قامت به الحرارة والبرودة يكون عاقلاً للحرارة والبرودة كـ (الجمادات) وإذا تحقق معنى الكلي في الأذهان فمنه ما هو موجود بعد الكثرة كالحاصل في النفس من معنى الإنسان عند حصول صورة زيد وعمرو في الخيال، ومنه ما هو موجود قبل الكثرة وهو ما يكون حصول المعاني الشخصية المطابقة له الأعيان متأخرة عن وجوده في العقل كالحاصل بالنسبة إلى الأنفس الفلكية وهو ما يعبر عنه قوم بالمثل، بل وكما بالنسبة إلى الواحد منا فإنه قد يتصور في نفسه معنى، ثم يحصل بعد ذلك من الأشخاص ما نسبته إليها نسبة ما لو كان مأخوذاً منها كان ذلك منسوباً إلى فعله أو فعل غيره، وليس ذلك مما لا يكون إلا جسماً كما ظن الرواقيون - بناءاً على اعتقادهم أن كل كلي جسم - إلا أن يكون عنايتهم بلفظ الجسم الوجود، إذ
And according to what we have established of the meaning of the universal, it cannot exist in individuals, otherwise that which is suitable for the occurrence of participation in it would be restricted to them and would not accept participation, which is impossible. And it is not as the Kalbiyun (Stoics) thought that the universal is a freely imagined thing, not subsistent and not existing. For even though it has no existence in external entities nor in individuals, it exists in the mind. For every rational person finds in himself and in his own self the existence of meanings such that if he related them to the particular meanings, they would correspond to those meanings, in the manner of the representation of the meaning of 'human', 'horse', etc. How so? Indeed we know that the individuals of a species like 'human', for example, are either identical in every respect or differ in every respect, or agree in some respect and differ in some respect. If they were identical in every respect, there would be no multiplicity, while they are multiple. If they differed in every respect, then if one of them is a human, it would be necessary that none of the remaining individuals be a human, which is a contradiction. So no alternative remains except that they agree in some respect and not in another. Thereupon it is possible to disregard the aspect of difference while conceiving the aspect of agreement, and that conceived something is the universal meaning. The meaning of the universal's existence in the mind is nothing other than that its quiddity (mahiyya) is distinguished in the rational soul—not in the sense that its form inheres in the rational being such that one who conceives heat and cold in a universal way would become hot or cold, or that everything in which heat and cold inhere conceives heat and cold, like inanimate objects. And when the reality of the universal in minds is established, among it there is that which exists after multiplicity, like what obtains in the soul of the meaning of 'human' upon obtaining the images of Zayd and 'Amr in the imagination. And among it there is that which exists before multiplicity, which is when the obtaining of the particular meanings corresponding to it from external entities is posterior to its existence in the mind, like what obtains in relation to the celestial souls, which some call 'exemplars' (al-muthul). Similarly, in relation to one of us: he may conceive in himself a meaning, and thereafter individuals obtain whose relation to it is the relation of what, if it had been taken from them, would be attributed to his action or the action of another. And this is not something that cannot be except a body, as the Stoics thought, based on their belief that every universal is a body—unless their concern with the word 'body' is 'existence', since
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage defends the reality of universals in the mind and refutes the Stoic position that universals are mere mental fictions (wahmiyyun mursal). The author begins by asserting that the universal cannot exist in external individuals, because if it did, that which is suitable for participation would be confined to those individuals, making further participation impossible. This contradicts the definition of universal as suitable for sharing. The author then explicitly denies the Stoic view, stating that although the universal has no external existence, it does exist in the mind. Every rational person finds within themselves the presence of universal meanings that correspond to particular instances. To prove this, the author presents a logical argument by considering the individuals of a species: they must be either completely identical, completely different, or partly identical and partly different. Complete identity would negate multiplicity, which is false because individuals are many. Complete difference would mean that if one individual is, say, human, then none of the others could be human, which is contradictory. Therefore, they must agree in some respect and differ in another. From this, we can abstract the aspect of agreement, disregarding differences, and that abstracted meaning is the universal. The author clarifies that mental existence does not mean the universal's form inheres in the rational soul such that conceiving heat would make one hot; rather, it means the quiddity (mahiyya) is mentally distinguished. Once mental existence is established, the author distinguishes two types: (1) universal after multiplicity (kulli ba'd al-kathra), like the concept of 'human' derived from perceiving Zayd and 'Amr, and (2) universal before multiplicity (kulli qabl al-kathra), where the universal is conceived first and then particular instances come later, as in the case of celestial souls (al-nufus al-falakiyya) or even human inventors who conceive a design before making individual artifacts. The passage ends by criticizing the Stoics for materializing universals, suggesting they used 'body' to mean existence itself.

Refuting the Stoics and Proving Mental Existence of Universals
Author refutes the Stoics' denial of universals and proves their existence in the mind.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's proof that universals exist in the intellect and refute the Stoic objection using the tripartite disjunction.

Core Idea
Universals exist neither as Platonic Forms nor as fantasies, but as abstracted meanings in the intellect.

The Stoics claimed that universals are mere imaginary concepts with no real existence. The author counters this by showing that universals must have some mode of existence—specifically, mental existence. He uses a logical argument from the nature of individuals within a species to prove that a common meaning must be abstracted by the mind. This lesson unpacks that argument and clarifies what 'exists in the mind' truly means.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Stoic position
Find where the author states the Stoic view: 'الكلي وهمي مرسل غير متقوم ولا موجود' – the universal is an imaginary concept, not self-subsistent nor existent.
2
Understand the author's counter-claim
The author asserts that universals exist in the intellect (موجود في العقل). He will prove this.
3
Analyze the tripartite disjunction
The author presents three possibilities for individuals of a species: (1) agree in every respect, (2) differ in every respect, (3) agree in some respects. This is an exhaustive and mutually exclusive division.
4
Apply reductio to the first two options
If individuals agreed in all respects, they would be identical (contradicts multiplicity). If they differed in all respects, only one could be e.g. 'man' (contradicts many men). Both are impossible, so the third option must hold: they agree in some respects.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
5
Extract the universal and clarify mental existence
The common aspect is the universal. The mind can disregard differentiating features and consider the shared meaning. Existing in the mind means the quiddity is distinguished in the intellect, not that it inheres physically (e.g., thinking of heat doesn't make you hot).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس كما ظن الكلبيين أن الكلي وهمي مرسل غير متقوم ولا موجود، فإنه وإن لم يكن له وجود في الأعيان ولا في الأشخاص فإنه موجود في العقل، فإن كل عاقل يجد من نفسه وفي نفسه وجود معان لو نسبها إلى المعاني الشخصية كانت مطابقة لمعانيها على نحو ما يتمثل من معنى الإنسان والفرس وغيره، كيف وإنَّا نعلم أن أشخاص نوع الإنسان مثلاً إما أن يتفق من كل وجه أو يفترق من كل وجه، أو تتفق من وجه وتفترق من وجه، فإن اتفقت من كل وجه فلا تَعدُّدَ وهي متعددة، وإن اختلفت من كل وجه فإن كان الواحد منها إنساناً وجب أن لا يكون شيء من باقي الأشخاص إنساناً وهو خلف، فلم يبق إلا أن تكون متفقة من وجه دون وجه وعند ذلك يخفى جواز قطع النظر عن ما به الافتراق مع تصور ما به الاتفاق وذلك المتصور هو المعنى بالكلي، وليس معنى كون الكلي موجوداً في العقل غير أن ماهيته متميزة في نفس العاقل لا بمعنى أن صورته قائمة بالعاقل حتى يقال إن مَنْ عقل الحرارة والبرودة تعقلاً كلياً يكون حاراً أو بارداً، أو إن كل من قامت به الحرارة والبرودة يكون عاقلاً للحرارة والبرودة كـ (الجمادات).
It is not as the Stoics thought that the universal is a purely imaginary concept, not self-subsistent nor existent. For although it has no existence in external entities nor in individuals, it does exist in the intellect. Indeed, every rational being finds in himself and within his soul the existence of meanings which, if compared to particular meanings, correspond to them, just as the meaning of man and horse and others is represented. Moreover, we know that the individuals of a species, for example man, either agree in every respect, or differ in every respect, or agree in some respects and differ in others. If they agree in every respect, then there is no multiplicity, yet they are multiple. If they differ in every respect, then if one of them is a man, it would necessarily mean that none of the remaining individuals is a man, which is self-contradictory. So it remains that they must agree in some respects without others. Then it becomes clear that it is permissible to disregard the differentiating aspects while considering the common aspect, and that considered aspect is the universal meaning. And the meaning of the universal's existence in the intellect is nothing other than its quiddity being distinguished in the mind of the rational being, not in the sense that its form inheres in the rational being such that one who conceives heat and cold universally would become hot or cold, or that everything in which heat and cold inhere would be a conceiver of heat and cold, like inanimate objects.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage refutes the Stoic view that universals are merely imaginary and non-existent. The author argues that universals exist in the mind. He provides a proof: individuals of a species must share some common aspect; otherwise, either they would be identical (contradicting multiplicity) or completely different (making the name 'man' unique to one individual). Since we have many individuals called 'man', they must share a common meaning, which is the universal. He then clarifies that existing in the mind does not mean the universal becomes a physical quality in the conceiver; otherwise, thinking of heat would make one hot. Thus, universals are abstract concepts.

الكلبيين

Classification of Universals: After and Before Multiplicity
Author classifies universals and refutes restriction to essential attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a universal after multiplicity and a universal before multiplicity, and explain why accidental attributes can also be universal.

Core Idea
Universality is about applicability to many, not about being essential or a substance.

The passage classifies universals into two types based on their relation to particulars, and then refutes the mistaken view that only essential attributes can be universal. Understanding this clarifies the scope of logical concepts in Islamic thought, showing that accidents like knowledge or whiteness are fully universal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two kinds of universals
Look for the phrases 'existing after multiplicity' (الكلي بعد الكثرة) and 'existing before multiplicity' (الكلي قبل الكثرة). The author defines each and gives examples.
2
Analyze the example of 'insan'
The universal 'man' (insan) is after multiplicity: we abstract it from seeing Zayd and Amr. Understand that it is derived from particular instances.
This is the common way we acquire universals.
3
Examine the refutation of restrictions
The author rejects the Stoic view that universals must be bodies, and rejects the view that only essential attributes are universal. Find the arguments: universality is based on suitability for participation, not essence.
4
Grasp the criterion for universality
The author states that universality is due to 'suitability for participation' (صلاحية لوقوع الاشتراك). This means any concept, whether essential or accidental, that can apply to many is universal.
This expands the scope of universals beyond the five predicables.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا تحقق معنى الكلي في الأذهان فمنه ما هو موجود بعد الكثرة كالحاصل في النفس من معنى الإنسان عند حصول صورة زيد وعمرو في الخيال، ومنه ما هو موجود قبل الكثرة وهو ما يكون حصول المعاني الشخصية المطابقة له الأعيان متأخرة عن وجوده في العقل كالحاصل بالنسبة إلى الأنفس الفلكية وهو ما يعبر عنه قوم بالمثل، بل وكما بالنسبة إلى الواحد منا فإنه قد يتصور في نفسه معنى، ثم يحصل بعد ذلك من الأشخاص ما نسبته إليها نسبة ما لو كان مأخوذاً منها كان ذلك منسوباً إلى فعله أو فعل غيره، وليس ذلك مما لا يكون إلا جسماً كما ظن الرواقيون - بناءاً على اعتقادهم أن كل كلي جسم - إلا أن يكون عنايتهم بلفظ الجسم الوجود، إذ الموجودات منها جواهر وأعراض كما يأتي، والكلي قد يتحقق بالنسبة وإلى كل واحد منها وليس الكلي مختصاً بما هو ذاتي أو مقول في طريق ما كما ظن بعضهم، إذ كليّة الشيء على ما تحقق إنما هي بسبب صلاحيته لوقوع الاشتراك فيه لا لكونه ذاتياً، وذلك لا فرق فيه بين الذاتيات والعرضيات كما يأتي.
And once the meaning of the universal is realized in minds, some of it exists after multiplicity, like what is obtained in the soul of the meaning of man when the images of Zayd and Amr are obtained in the imagination. And some of it exists before multiplicity, which is where the particular meanings corresponding to it in external entities are subsequent to its existence in the intellect, like what is obtained with respect to the celestial souls, which is what some people call 'ideas'. Moreover, as with respect to each one of us, one may conceive in himself a meaning, then afterwards from among individuals there comes what has a relation to it such that if it were taken from them, that would be attributed to his action or the action of another. And this is not something that can only be a body, as the Stoics thought, based on their belief that every universal is a body — unless their intention with the word 'body' is 'existence', since existents include substances and accidents as will come. The universal can be realized with respect to each one of them, and the universal is not specific to what is essential or predicated in a certain way, as some thought. Indeed, the universality of a thing, as verified, is only due to its suitability for participation, not because it is essential. And there is no difference in this between essential and accidental attributes, as will come.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
Here the author classifies universals into two kinds: (1) those that exist after multiplicity, i.e., abstracted from particular experiences, like the concept of man derived from seeing Zayd and Amr; and (2) those that exist before multiplicity, i.e., concepts that precede their instances, like Platonic forms or the ideas of celestial souls. He also refers to the Stoics who thought universals must be bodies, but refutes this, noting that existence includes both substances and accidents. Then he emphasizes that universality is not restricted to essential predicates; accidental attributes can also be universal, since universality is based on suitability for multiple instances.

الكلي بعد الكثرة
الكلي قبل الكثرة

Definition of the Particular and Its Accidental Nature
Author defines the particular and explains that particularity is not intrinsic but accidental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the particular (al-juz’ī) according to the logicians and explain why particularity is an accidental attribute.

Core Idea
Particularity is not about being sensible; it is about being unable to apply to many.

This passage from Daqā’iq al-Ḥaqā’iq defines the particular and clears up a common misunderstanding. Many assume that particulars must be physical objects, but the author shows that even non-sensible things like individual souls are particulars. Furthermore, he argues that the properties 'particular' and 'universal' are not intrinsic—they are accidental. Let’s break down the definition and reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
Find the phrase that defines the particular. It is: 'الذي لا يصلح مفهومه لاشتراك كثيرين فيه' – 'whose concept is not apt for the participation of many in it.' This is the logical definition.
2
Recognize the sensibility clarification
The author stresses that particularity is not due to being sensible. He contrasts sensible individuals (Zayd, Amr) with non‑sensible ones (the soul of Zayd). Both are particulars because they cannot be shared.
This counters the mistaken view that only physical objects are particulars.
3
Understand the accidental nature argument
The author says that if particularity and universality were essential, a thing would have to be both (impossible) or always one. Since that is not the case, they must be accidental attributes coming from outside.
This is a reductio argument: if intrinsic, contradictions arise.
4
Note the second sense of 'particular'
The passage begins to describe another meaning: a particular can also be something that can be predicated of itself and of another universal. The text is cut off, but this hints at a broader usage.
This second sense may refer to lower universals that act as particulars relative to higher ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجزئي فقد عرف أنه الذي لا يصلح مفهومه لاشتراك كثيرين فيه، ولا فرق في ذلك بين أن يكون محسوساً لزيد وعمرو أو غير محسوس كنفس زيد ونفس عمرو، إذ الجزئية ليست بسبب كون الشيء محسوساً بل لعدم قبوله للشركة، وذلك قد يكون في ما ليس بمحسوس كما في نفس زيد فإنه لا يتصور اشتراك كثيرين فيها من حيث هي نفس زيد، إلا أن يقطع النظر خصوصها بزيد، فإنها إذ ذاك تكون كلية لا جزئية، وليس ما حققناه من معنى الكلي والجزئي ثابتاً لذات ما ثابتاً لذات ما سمي جزئياً أو كلياً وإلا لما وجد ذلك المعنى إلا كلياً، أو لما وجد إلا جزئياً، أو أن يكون كلياً وجزئياً معاً وهو محال، فإذن ليس ذلك له إلا بعارض من خارج، وقد يستعمل الجزئي باعتبار آخر وهو كل ما يصلح أن يقال عليه وعلى غيره كلي آخر، ولا فرق في ذلك الغير بين
As for the particular, it has been defined as that whose concept is not apt for the participation of many in it. There is no difference in this between whether it is sensible like Zayd and Amr or non-sensible like the soul of Zayd and the soul of Amr, since particularity is not due to a thing being sensible but due to its not accepting participation. And that can occur in what is not sensible, as in the soul of Zayd, for it is inconceivable that many participate in it insofar as it is the soul of Zayd, unless one disregards its specification to Zayd; then it becomes universal, not particular. And what we have verified of the meaning of universal and particular is not fixed to the essence of what is called particular or universal. Otherwise, that meaning would only be found as universal, or only as particular, or would be both universal and particular together, which is impossible. So it is not so except by an external accident. And the particular is sometimes used in another sense: everything that is apt to be predicated of itself and of another universal. And there is no difference in that other...
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage defines the particular (juz'i) as a concept that cannot apply to many. The author emphasizes that particularity is not tied to being sensible; non-sensible entities like the soul of Zayd are also particular because they cannot be shared. However, if we abstract from the specific individual, the concept becomes universal. He then notes that the properties of universality and particularity are not intrinsic; they are accidental (from outside). Finally, he mentions another sense of particular: something that can be predicated of itself and of another universal, which is like an accidental universal that can be predicated of multiple things. The passage ends mid-sentence.

The Criterion of Universality is Suitability for Sharing
Author defines universality as suitability for sharing and rejects essentiality as a condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct criterion for universality and distinguish it from the mistaken criterion based on essentiality.

Core Idea
Universality depends on potential sharing, not on being essential.

The author clarifies that the universal (al-kulli) is not restricted to essential predicates. Instead, any concept that can be shared by multiple individuals qualifies as universal, whether it is essential or accidental. This lesson examines the definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Locate the author's definition of universal
In the Arabic, find the phrase 'صلاحيته لوقوع الاشتراك فيه' which means 'suitability for the occurrence of sharing in it'. This is the central criterion.
2
Identify what the author rejects as criteria
The author explicitly rejects two alternatives: being essential (dhati) and being said in a certain way (maqul fi tariq ma). Underline these phrases.
The refutation of 'said in a certain way' is taken up in the next lesson.
3
Apply the criterion to an example
Consider the concept 'walking'. Is it a universal? According to the author, yes, because multiple individuals can share in walking, even though it is accidental to humans.
Essential predicates like 'human' also satisfy the criterion, but so do accidental ones.
4
Contrast with the mistaken view
Some thought that only essential predicates are universals because they enter the definition. But the author says universality is about sharing, not definitional status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموجودات منها جواهر وأعراض كما يأتي، والكلي قد يتحقق بالنسبة إلى كل واحد منها وليس الكلي مختصاً بما هو ذاتي أو مقول في طريق ما كما ظن بعضهم، إذ كليّة الشيء على ما تحقق إنما هي بسبب صلاحيته لوقوع الاشتراك فيه لا لكونه ذاتياً، وذلك لا فرق فيه بين الذاتيات والعرضيات كما يأتي. وما قيل في بيان أن كل كلي مقول في طريق ما من أن يمشي يحمل على زيد وليس يمشي كلياً لزيد، فإن زيداً غير جزئي، لماذا لا يقال عليه في طريق ما؟ وكل ما لا يقال عليه في طريق ما فليس بكلي فقط من جهة أن زيداً وإن كان جزئياً بالنسبة إلى نوع الإنسان، فمن حيث ينتقل برفع قدم ووضع أخرى جزئي بالنسبة إلى مطلق مفهوم يمشي، إذ هو يطلق على زيد وغيره من الأشخاص المنتقلين من حيث هم كذلك الكيف، وأن المطلوب كل كلي مقول في طريق ما، والمستعمل في بيانه من المقدمة الكبرى، وهي ما ليس مقولاً في طريق ما ليس بكلي عكس النقيض، ولو كان بيناً لكان المطلوب بيناً. ولا يخفى أن تعريف غير البين بما هو غير بين ممتنع.
Existing things among them are substances and accidents as will come, and the universal may be realized with respect to each one of them, and the universal is not exclusive to what is essential or said in a certain way as some thought, because the universality of a thing, as established, is only due to its suitability for the occurrence of sharing in it, not because it is essential, and there is no difference in that between essentials and accidents as will come. And what was said in explaining that every universal is said in a certain way, that 'walks' is predicated of Zayd and 'walks' is not a universal for Zayd, for Zayd is not particular, why is it not said of him in a certain way? And everything that is not said of it in a certain way is not universal, only from the aspect that Zayd, although he is particular relative to the species human, from the aspect that he moves by lifting one foot and placing another, he is particular relative to the absolute concept of walking, because it is applied to Zayd and others of the moving persons in that respect. And the required is that every universal is said in a certain way, and what is used in its explanation from the major premise, namely 'what is not said in a certain way is not universal', is the converse of the contrapositive, and if it were evident, the required would be evident. And it is not hidden that defining the non-evident by what is non-evident is impossible.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage defines the universal (al-kulli) and corrects a common misunderstanding. The author begins by stating that universality is not restricted to essential (dhati) predicates or to things said 'in a certain way' (fi tariq ma), as some mistakenly held. Instead, universality is solely a function of the concept's suitability for multiple instantiation (salahiyyatuhu li-wuqu' al-ishtirak fihi). This applies equally to essential and accidental predicates. The author then refutes a specific argument that attempted to prove that every universal is said in a certain way. The argument claimed that 'walks' (yamshi) is predicated of Zayd but is not a universal for Zayd because Zayd is particular (juz'i). However, the author points out that Zayd is particular relative to the species human, but relative to the concept of walking, he is particular only in a different sense; 'walks' applies to Zayd and others, so it is a universal. The argument's major premise—'what is not said in a certain way is not universal'—is actually the converse of the contrapositive of the desired claim, and if it were self-evident (bayyin), the conclusion would also be self-evident, which it is not. Thus, the argument fails. The author concludes that defining something non-evident by something non-evident is invalid. The key takeaway is that universality is defined by potential sharing, not by mode of predication.

كلي
مقول في طريق ما
ذاتي
عرضي

A) That it is an essential predicate of its subject.
B) That it is said of its subject in a certain way.
C) That its meaning is suitable for multiple individuals to share in it.
D) That it is a genus or species.

Key Takeaway
Universality is defined solely by a concept's suitability for multiple instantiation, not by its essentiality or mode of predication.

Refuting the Criterion of Predication in a Certain Mode
Author refutes the view that universality depends on being said in a certain way.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that 'every universal is said in a certain way' and identify its logical flaw of circular reasoning.

Core Idea
The claim that every universal is said in a certain way leads to circular reasoning.

The author refutes a specific argument that attempted to prove that every universal is said in a certain way (maqul fi tariq ma). By analyzing the argument's structure, we uncover that it defines the non-evident by the non-evident, a logical error. This lesson walks you through the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being attacked
The opponents claim: 'Every universal is said in a certain way.' Find this in the Arabic: 'أن كل كلي مقول في طريق ما'.
2
Extract the opponents' argument
The opponents argue using the major premise: 'What is not said in a certain way is not universal'. This premise is the contrapositive of the conclusion. The minor premise is that 'walks' is predicated of Zayd in a certain way.
The author notes that the major premise is the converse of the contrapositive (عكس النقيض).
3
Identify the logical flaw
The author points out that the argument is circular: the major premise is logically equivalent to the conclusion. Therefore, the argument assumes what it needs to prove.
This is called 'musadara 'ala al-matlub' (begging the question).
4
Apply the author's conclusion
The criterion for universality is not 'said in a certain way' but suitability for sharing. The attempted proof fails.
