Every Species Needs a Constitutive Differentia
Author refutes objections and proves necessity of constitutive differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof that every species under a genus requires a constitutive differentia, distinguishing it from an accidental distinguisher.

Core Idea
A species is only a species if its differentia constitutes its essence.

This passage tackles objections to the claim that every species must have a constitutive differentia. The author first responds to two subtle objections, then lays out a crisp dichotomous proof. By following the argument, you will see why an accidental feature cannot establish a genuine genus-species relation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two objections
The author addresses objections about genus being matter and about a simple being producing two effects (constitutiveness and divisiveness). He clarifies that genus differs from matter, and that different aspects or order of priority allow a single simple to have both effects.
2
Identify the main claim under attack
The claim is: 'Every species under a genus must have a constitutive differentia.' The objector asks why this is necessary.
3
Follow the dichotomous proof
The author considers two cases: either the species is distinguished from the genus by something, or it is not. If not, there is no genus-species relation. If yes, that distinguisher is either constitutive or accidental.
4
Examine the accidental distinguisher case
If the distinguisher is accidental (like an accident distinguishing individuals), it does not create a genus-species difference. Thus, the more general would not be a true genus nor the more specific a true species.
5
Conclude that the distinguisher must be constitutive
Since the accidental case fails, the only remaining possibility is that the distinguisher is constitutive of the species. This proves the necessity of a constitutive differentia.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الإشكال الثاني: فإنما يلزم أن يكون لو كان الجسم المشاهد في الحيوان والنبات هو الجنس المخصص وليس كذلك، بل هو المادة، وفرق بين الجنس والمادة كما سبق.
وإما الإشكال الثالث: فإنما يلزم أن لو قيل بصدور الأثرين عن البسيط الواحد من جهة واحدة، وما المانع من أن يكون صدور التقويم والتقسيم عنه باعتبار جهتين مختلفتين لا توجب في ذاته تعدداً ولا كثرة كما يأتي تحقيقه، أو أن صدور أحد الأثرين عنه لازم عنه لذاته وصدور الآخر عنه بواسطة صدوره عن لازم ذاته، ويكون أحدهما سابقاً على الآخر، أما التقسيم أو التقويم، كيف وأن صفة التقويم والتقسيم نسبة وإضافة والبسيط الواحد لا يمتنع أن يتصف بالنسب المتعددة والإضافات المختلفة كما سبق في اتصاف الوحدة بكونها نصف الإثنين وثلث الثلاثة وغير ذلك من النسب.
وقوله: (لِمَ قلتم إن كل نوع تحت جنس لا بد له من فصل مقوم... إلى آخره). قلنا: لأنه لا يخلو إما أن يمتاز نوع الجنس عن الجنس بما تخصص عنه أو لا يمتاز ، فإن كان الثاني فلا امتياز لنوع الجنس عن الجنس، وعند ذلك فليس أحدهما جنساً للآخر، والآخر نوعاً أولى من العكس، وإن كان الأول فذلك المميز المخصص إما أن يكون مقوماً لحقيقة النوع أو لا يكون مقوماً لها، فإن لم يكن مقوماً لحقيقة النوع فهو مميز عارض والمميز العارض لا يوجب المخالفة بين الأعم والأخص بالجنسية والنوعية كامتياز الأصناف والأشخاص عن الأنواع المقولة عليها، ولا يكون الأعم جنساً ولا الأخض نوعاً، فلم يبق إلا أن يكون مقوماً وهو المطلوب.
As for the second objection: it would only follow if the body observed in animal and plant were the specific genus, but it is not; rather it is matter, and there is a difference between genus and matter as previously. As for the third objection: it would only follow if it were said that two effects proceed from a single simple being from one aspect, but what prevents the issuance of constitutiveness and divisiveness from it considering two different aspects that do not cause plurality or multiplicity in its essence, as its verification will come, or that one of the two effects proceeds from it necessarily from its essence and the other proceeds from it by way of proceeding from a necessary concomitant of its essence, and one of them is prior to the other, whether it is divisiveness or constitutiveness, especially since the attribute of constitutiveness and divisiveness is a relation and addition, and a single simple being is not prevented from being described with multiple relations and different additions, as previously in the description of unity as being half of two and one-third of three and other such relations. And His statement: (Why did you say that every species under a genus must have a constitutive differentia... etc.). We say: because it is either that the species of the genus is distinguished from the genus by what it is specified by, or it is not distinguished. If the second, then there is no distinction of the species of the genus from the genus, and thus one of them is not a genus for the other, and the other is a species, rather the opposite. If the first, then that distinguishing specifier is either constitutive of the reality of the species or not constitutive of it. If it is not constitutive of the reality of the species, then it is an accidental distinguishing feature, and the accidental distinguishing feature does not necessitate difference between the more general and the more specific in terms of genus-species, like the distinction of varieties and individuals from the species that are predicated of them, nor is the more general a genus nor the more specific a species. So nothing remains except that it be constitutive, which is what is sought.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a commentary addressing objections and then proving that every species must have a constitutive differentia. The author first handles two objections regarding the relationship between genus and differentia. In the second objection, someone might think that the body observed in animals and plants is the genus that specifies, but the author clarifies that it is matter, not genus—genus and matter are distinct. In the third objection, it is asked how a simple being can produce two effects (constitutiveness and divisiveness). The author responds that these effects can arise from different aspects or through one being necessary and the other a consequence of a necessary concomitant, just as a single unity can be described as half of two and third of three. Then the author turns to the query: 'Why do you say every species under a genus must have a constitutive differentia?' The proof proceeds by dichotomy: either the species is distinguished from the genus by something that specifies it or not. If not distinguished, then there is no genus-species relation. If distinguished, then the distinguisher is either constitutive or accidental. If accidental, it does not create the genus-species difference, like accidental distinctions among individuals. Therefore, the distinguisher must be constitutive, which is the required conclusion.

A) Because otherwise the species would be indistinguishable from the genus.
B) Because all species have accidents that distinguish them.

Key Takeaway
A species is defined by a constitutive differentia that makes it what it is; accidental features do not create true species.

Proper Accident and Common Accident: Conditions for Definition
Division of accident into proper and common accident, with conditions for logical proper accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish proper accident from common accident and identify which proper accidents are useful in definitions.

Core Idea
Only the coextensive permanent proper accident is useful for defining species.

In this passage, the author divides accident into two types: proper accident (specific to one universal) and common accident (predicable of many). He then focuses on the conditions that make a proper accident logically useful for definitions. Understanding this distinction is key to crafting sound definitions in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basis of division
The author divides accident based on whether it is predicated of one universal or of many. Note that accident here means non-essential predicate.
2
Define proper accident (الخاصة)
It is a non-essential predicate of one universal. But not all are useful; the logical proper accident must be permanent for all individuals of the species and coextensive with it, like the capacity for laughter for humans.
3
Compare with common accident (العرض العام)
It is predicated of many differing essences, non-essentially. It may be permanent (e.g., blackness of raven) or temporary.
4
Recognize why proper accident definition does not cover common accident
Unlike differentia, a proper accident can be non-coextensive; thus a separate definition is needed for common accident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثامن
في قسمة العرضي إلى الخاصة والعرض العام وتحقيق
كل واحد منهما
وإنما نقسم العرضي إليهما لأنه إما أن يكون مقولاً على كليّ ،واحد أو على كليات متعددة أكثر من واحد، فإن كان الأول فهو الخاصة، وإن كان الثاني فهو العرض العام، وأول مبدوء به إنما هو الأنفع منهما وهو الخاصة دون العرض العام، إذ العرض العام قلّما ينتفع به في العلوم.
والخاصة قد تطلق على ما يقال على كلي واحد قولاً غير ذاتي، لكن منها ما هو مقول على جميع جزئيات ذلك المعنى الكلي، ومنها ما يقال على بعضها، وكل واحد من الأمرين منه دائم، ومنه مفارق، فما هو دائم للكلي فكقوة الضحك والكتابة للإنسان، وما هو للكلي غير دائم في جميع الأوقات فكالشباب والشيخوخة بالنسبة إلى الإنسان وما هو ثابت للبعض مع الدوام فكالفطوسة في أنف الإنسان، وما هو ثابت للبعض من غير دوام فكالكتابة بالفعل والضحك بالنسبة إلى الإنسان.
والخاصة المنطقية النافعة في صناعة الرسوم ما كانت دائمة لكل أشخاص النوع، وهي بحيث تكون هي والنوع متعاكسين كما في قوة الكتابة والضحك لنوع الإنسان، وما سوى ذلك فلا، إذ هو مما لا ينتفع به في الرسوم؛ لعدم مساواته للشيء المرسوم، وانتفاء القول بتعاكسهما، والمميز يجب أن يكون مساوياً لما ميزه وإلا كان المميز أعم مما ميزه أو أخص منه، والتعريف به إذ ذاك محال. وأما العرض العام فهو ما يقال على كثيرين مختلفين بالذوات قولاً غير ذاتي وسواء كان ذلك دائماً كـ (سواد الغراب والقار أو غير دائم كـ (المشي بالفعل) بالنسبة إلى الإنسان والفرس فالقيد الأوّل ميزه عن الأجناس وهو وإن اتفق إن كان مع عمومه للأنواع خاصة لما فوقها كـ (الحركة فإنها خاصة للحيوان وعرض عام للإنسان والفرس، فتعريف الخاصة غير كاف عن تعريفه كما كان تعريف الفصل مطلقاً كافياً عن تعريف ما وقع عاماً من فصول الأجناس، وذلك لأن ما به وقع الاختلاف بين الفصول إنما هو في أمر خارج عن كونها فصولاً من جهة أن بعضها قد يكون فصلاً للجنس، وبعضها فصلاً للنوع، وعموم فصل الجنس لا يخرجه عن أن يكون فصلاً للجنس، فتعريف الفصل من حيث هو فصل كاف عن تعريف جهاته، وليس تعريف الخاصة من حيث هي خاصة مغنياً عن تعريف العرض العام فإنه وإن اتفق إن كان خاصة فقد يتفق أن لا يكون خاصة كـ (الوجود) بالنسبة إلى الجوهر والعرض، فإنه عرض عام وليس بخاصة لشيء، وإلا كان فوقها ما هو أعم منها ولا أعم منها غيره، والشيء لا يكون خاصة نفسه، فإذن تعريف الخاصة مما لا يندرج فيه ما قيل بكونه عرضاً عاماً، وقد يتفق أن ما كان عرضاً عاماً بالنسبة إلى أشياء خاصة بالنسبة إلى ما فوقها، أو ما كان خاصة لشيء عارض عام لما تحته، إذ لا يمتنع أن ما كان خاصة لأعمّ كلتين أن يكون عرضاً عاماً بالنسبة إلى أخصهما، بل ولا يمتنع أن يكون جنساً لخاصة الأخص أيضاً.
وأعلم أن ما أشرنا إلى تحقيقه من قسمي العرض إنما هو على ما اقتضته الخاصة وإلا فالمشهور عن الأوائل أنهم كانوا يقسمون العرض إلى ما هو لازم دائم لكلية كلي واحد دون غيره، وإلى ما ليس كذلك، فما كان من الأول خصوه باسم الخاصة، وما كان من الثاني أبقوه على إطلاقه وسموه (عرضاً) وسواء كان ذلك خاصاً بكلي واحد أو أكثر، وسواء كان دائماً أو مفارقاً كما سبق تعريفه، حتى إنهم كانوا يدرجون في ذلك كتابة الإنسان وضحكه بالفعل، ولا حرج في الاصطلاح بعد فهم المعنى.
وإن تشوّقت إلى رسم الخاصة والعرض على مصطلحهم، فالخاصة ما هو عارض لجميع جزئيات كلي واحد وحده دائماً، وهو كما أشرنا إليه الخاصية المنطقية سابقاً.
Chapter Eight: On the division of accident into proper accident and common accident, and the verification of each of them. We divide accident into them because it is either predicated of one universal or of multiple universals more than one. If the first, it is the proper accident; if the second, it is the common accident. We begin with the more useful of them, which is the proper accident, not the common accident, because the common accident is rarely useful in the sciences. The proper accident may be applied to what is said of one universal non-essentially, but of it some is predicated of all particulars of that universal meaning, and some is predicated of some of them; each of the two matters is either permanent or separable. What is permanent for the universal is like the capacity for laughter and writing for man; what is not permanent for the universal at all times is like youth and old age in relation to man; what is constant for some with permanence is like a snub nose in a man's nose; what is constant for some without permanence is like actual writing and laughter in relation to man. The logical proper accident that is useful in the craft of definitions is that which is permanent for every individual of the species, such that it and the species are coextensive, as in the capacity for writing and laughter for the human species. Other than that is not, because it is not useful in definitions due to lack of equality with the thing defined and the absence of coextensiveness. The distinguisher must be equal to what it distinguishes; otherwise the distinguisher would be more general or more specific than what it distinguishes, and definition by it would be impossible. As for the common accident, it is that which is said of many things differing in essence non-essentially, whether it is permanent like blackness of the raven and pitch, or not permanent like actual walking in relation to man and horse. The first condition distinguishes it from genera. And although it may happen that with its generality over species it is a proper accident for what is above it, like motion, for it is a proper accident for animal and a common accident for man and horse. So the definition of proper accident is not sufficient for its definition, as the definition of differentia absolutely was sufficient for defining what occurs as common among differentiae of genera. This is because the difference between the differentiae lies in something external to being differentiae, in that some are differentiae of the genus and some are differentiae of the species, and the generality of the differentia of the genus does not remove it from being a differentia of the genus. Thus the definition of differentia insofar as it is differentia is sufficient for defining its aspects. But the definition of proper accident insofar as it is proper accident does not suffice for defining the common accident. For although it may happen to be a proper accident, it may also happen not to be a proper accident, like existence in relation to substance and accident, for it is a common accident and not a proper accident for anything; otherwise there would be above it something more general, and there is nothing more general than it, and a thing cannot be a proper accident of itself. So the definition of proper accident does not include what is said to be a common accident. And it may happen that what is a common accident for things is a proper accident for what is above them, or what is a proper accident for a thing is a common accident for what is under it, for it is not impossible that what is a proper accident for the more general of two universals be a common accident for the more specific of them; nay, it is not impossible that it be a genus for the proper accident of the more specific as well. Know that what we have indicated regarding the verification of the two divisions of accident is according to what the specialty required. Otherwise, the well-known from the ancients is that they used to divide accident into what is necessarily permanent for all of one universal to the exclusion of others, and into what is not like that. What was of the first they distinguished by the name 'proper accident' and what was of the second they left general and called 'accident', whether it is specific to one universal or more, whether permanent or separable, as previously defined, to the extent that they included in that the writing and actual laughter of man. There is no harm in terminology after understanding the meaning. If you desire the definition of proper accident and accident according to their terminology: the proper accident is what is incidental to all particulars of one universal alone permanently, and it is as we have indicated, the logical property earlier.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes two types of accident (العرضي): proper accident (الخاصة) and common accident (العرض العام). The author begins by explaining the basis of division: an accident is either predicated of one universal or of many. He then defines proper accident as what is said of one universal non-essentially, and elaborates that it can be permanent or temporary, and for all or some particulars. However, for logical purposes (useful in definitions), the proper accident must be permanent for every individual of the species and coextensive with it, like the capacity for writing and laughter for humans. This ensures that the distinguisher equals the distinguished, which is necessary for a valid definition. Next, common accident is defined as what is said of many differing essences non-essentially, whether permanent (like blackness of raven) or temporary (like walking for man). The author notes that something can be a proper accident for a higher genus but a common accident for lower species, e.g., motion is proper to animal but common to man and horse. He then argues that the definition of proper accident does not suffice for common accident, unlike the case of differentia, because proper accident can be non-coextensive. Finally, he contrasts this logical division with the ancient practice: they divided accident into necessary permanent (proper accident) and not necessary (simply 'accident'), which could be specific or not. The author concludes by giving the ancient definition of proper accident as what is incidental to all particulars of one universal alone permanently.

Key Takeaway
A proper accident is useful in definition only when it is permanent for all individuals of the species and coextensive with it.

Division of 'Aradi: Khassa and 'Arad 'Amm
Divides 'aradi into khassa and 'arad 'amm and critiques two definitions of 'arad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between khassa and 'arad 'amm based on necessity and exclusivity, and identify examples of each.

Core Idea
Khassa is a constant accident of all individuals of one universal only; 'arad 'amm is not exclusive.

In this section, the author explains the division of accident ('aradi) into special characteristic (khassa) and general accident ('arad 'amm) according to the ancient logicians. Understanding this division is crucial for properly classifying attributes in definitions and descriptions. This lesson walks you through the definitions and examples provided in the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of accident
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where the author states that the ancients divided accident into two: what is a necessary constant attribute for a single universal exclusively, and what is not like that. The first is called khassa, the second retains the name 'arad.
2
Define khassa
The definition of khassa is: 'what is an accident of all individuals of a single universal alone constantly.' This means it must be present in every individual of that universal and must be exclusive to that universal.
The author notes that this is the logical property (khassiya) mentioned earlier.
3
Define 'arad 'amm
General accident is what does not meet the conditions of khassa: it may be specific to one universal or more, and it may be constant or separable. The author gives examples: man's writing and his actual laughter.
The term 'arad 'amm is used for all accidents that are not khassa.
4
Apply the definitions
Test the examples: Is writing a khassa for man? No, because not all men write (not constant) and some non-humans write (not exclusive). Is blackness a khassa for crow? No, because blackness is not exclusive to crows (many black things). So both are 'arad 'amm.
The author also mentions that the ancients had no issue with terminology after understanding the meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأعلم أن ما أشرنا إلى تحقيقه من قسمي العرض إنما هو على ما اقتضته الخاصة وإلا فالمشهور عن الأوائل أنهم كانوا يقسمون العرض إلى ما هو لازم دائم لكلية كلي واحد دون غيره، وإلى ما ليس كذلك، فما كان من الأول خصوه باسم الخاصة، وما كان من الثاني أبقوه على إطلاقه وسموه (عرضاً) وسواء كان ذلك خاصاً بكلي واحد أو أكثر، وسواء كان دائماً أو مفارقاً كما سبق تعريفه، حتى إنهم كانوا يدرجون في ذلك كتابة الإنسان وضحكه بالفعل، ولا حرج في الاصطلاح بعد فهم المعنى. وإن تشوّقت إلى رسم الخاصة والعرض على مصطلحهم، فالخاصة ما هو عارض لجميع جزئيات كلي واحد وحده دائماً، وهو كما أشرنا إليه الخاصية المنطقية سابقاً. الفصل الثامن: في قسمة العرضي إلى الخاصة والعرض العام وأما العرض فقد قيل فيه ما ليس بجنس ولا نوع ولا فصل ولا خاصة، قائم في الموضوع. وقيل هو ما يكون يفسد من غير فساد حامله وفي ما بين العبارتين تقصر، أما الأولى فلأنا قد بينا أن العرض ليس هو قسيم الجوهر، وهو ما وجوده في موضوع، بل قد يكون العرضي جوهراً كما سبق تحقيقه، وإذ ذاك فلا يكون الرسم جامعاً. كيف وأن تعريف الشيء بسلب حقيقة غيره عنه بعيد لعدم الأولوية، وأن قدر أن سلب ما أخذ في التعريف أظهر كان الاقتصار عليه كافياً، وهو أن يقال هو ما ليس بجنس ولا نوع ولا فصل ولا خاصة ضرورة الانحصار، وأن ما أخذ في التعريف أظهر. وأما العبارة الثالثة فإنها أيضاً غير جامعة إذ العارض منه ما هو دائم في الوجود غير مفارق كسواد الغراب والفطوسة للأنف ونحوه، ولا يفسد إلا وقد فسد حامله وإلا لكان مفارقاً نعم، لو أخذ ذلك بالنظر إلى الوهم كان صحيحاً، فإنه لا يلزم فساد الغراب عند توهم كونه غير أسود، فإنه من حيث هو هو ليس مما يتوقف على كونه أسود أو أبيض أو غير ذلك، لكنه مع ذلك بعيد عن ظاهر اللفظ ومتوقف في فهمه على أمر زائد، وذلك مما تأباه صناعة المنطقيين في الحدود والرسوم.
And know that what we indicated regarding the verification of the two divisions of accident is only according to what the special characteristic required. Otherwise, the famous view of the ancients is that they used to divide accident into what is a necessary constant attribute for every single universal exclusively, and into what is not like that. What was of the first they named 'special characteristic', and what was of the second they left it unrestricted and called it 'accident', whether that was specific to one universal or more, and whether constant or separable, as its definition preceded. They even included in that the writing of man and his actual laughter, and there is no harm in terminology after understanding the meaning. And if you desire a definition of the special characteristic and accident according to their terminology, the special characteristic is what is an accident of all individuals of a single universal alone constantly, and it is, as we indicated, the logical property earlier. Chapter Eight: On the Division of Accident into Special Characteristic and General Accident. As for accident, it has been said that it is what is not a genus, nor a species, nor a differentia, nor a special characteristic, subsisting in the subject. And it has been said that it is what perishes without the corruption of its bearer. Between the two expressions there is a shortcoming. As for the first, we have shown that accident is not the correlative of substance, which is what exists in a subject; rather, the accident could be a substance as verified previously, and then the definition is not comprehensive. Moreover, defining a thing by negating the reality of something else from it is far because of lack of priority, and if it is assumed that the negation taken in the definition is more obvious, it would be sufficient to limit oneself to it, and that is to say: it is what is not a genus, nor a species, nor a differentia, nor a special characteristic, due to the necessity of enumeration, and what is taken in the definition is more obvious. As for the third expression, it is also not comprehensive, because from the accident is what is constant in existence, non-separable, like the blackness of the crow and the cat-like nose and the like, and it does not perish except when its bearer perishes, otherwise it would be separable. Yes, if that is taken with consideration to the imagination, it is correct, because it is not necessary that the crow perish when imagining it being non-black, for in itself it is not dependent on being black or white or other than that. However, with that it is far from the apparent meaning and dependent in its understanding on an additional matter, and that is repugnant to the craft of logicians in definitions and descriptions.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage begins by clarifying a point about the division of accident ('arad). The author states that the earlier discussion (possibly referring to a previous section) about the two types of accident was according to the concept of 'special characteristic' (khassa). However, the famous view of the ancient logicians is different: they divide accident into that which is a necessary and constant attribute of a single universal exclusively (i.e., the property that belongs to all individuals of that universal and only them) and into that which is not like that. The first they named 'special characteristic' (khassa), and the second they left with the general name 'accident' ('arad), regardless of whether it is specific to one universal or more, and whether it is constant (like the blackness of a crow) or separable (like writing). They even include examples like man's writing and actual laughter. The author then provides the definition of khassa according to their terminology: it is what is an accident of all individuals of a single universal alone constantly. This is the logical property (khassiya) mentioned earlier.

The passage then continues with the chapter heading 'On the Division of Accident into Special Characteristic and General Accident'. Here, the author presents two definitions of accident ('arad) that were commonly given. The first definition is negative: 'accident is what is not a genus, not a species, not a differentia, and not a special characteristic, subsisting in the subject.' The second definition is: 'it is what perishes without the corruption of its bearer.' The author critiques both.

Regarding the first definition, the author points out two problems. First, accident is not simply the correlative of substance (jawhar) in the sense of 'what exists in a subject', because sometimes an accident can be a substance (as previously verified). Thus the definition is not comprehensive (jami'). Second, defining something by negating the reality of something else is problematic due to lack of priority. If the negation is more obvious, it would suffice to simply say 'it is not genus, not species, not differentia, not special characteristic' due to the necessary enumeration (implies that these five universals exhaust the categories, so the negation serves as a definition by exhaustion). But this is still not a proper definition.

As for the third expression (the second definition), the author says it is also not comprehensive. Why? Because some accidents are constant in existence and non-separable, such as the blackness of a crow or the cat-like nose of a certain nose shape. These do not perish unless their bearer perishes. So the definition 'perishes without corruption of bearer' does not cover constant accidents. The author notes that if one considers this definition with respect to the imagination (wahm), it could be valid: imagining the crow not black does not require the crow to perish. However, this is far from the apparent meaning of the definition and requires additional understanding, which is against the craft of logicians in definitions and descriptions.

In summary, the passage teaches that the division of 'aradi into khassa and 'arad 'amm is based on necessity and exclusivity, and it critiques two faulty definitions of 'arad, showing why they fail to capture all instances.

Key Terms
خاصة (khāṣṣa): A special characteristic (property) that is a necessary and constant accident of all individuals of a single universal exclusively. [Note: In some texts, khassa is used interchangeably with 'property' as one of the five universals; here it is specifically the 'logical property' as per the logicians' terminology.]
عرض عام (ʿaraḍ ʿāmm): A general accident that is not necessarily exclusive to a single universal; it can be present in multiple universals, and it can be either constant or separable.
عرض (ʿaraḍ): In the context of the five universals, it is the accident as a category; here it is the subject of division into khassa and 'arad 'amm. The passage critiques two definitions: one by negation of other universals, and one by perishability without bearer. [Note: In earlier Aristotelian logic, 'arad is often defined as 'what exists in a subject', but here the author shows that this definition conflates with substance.]
قيام في الموضوع (qiyām fī al-mawḍūʿ): Subsisting in the subject, i.e., existing as an accident in a substance. The author notes that this is insufficient because some 'aradi are substances. [Note: This is a standard definition of accident in Peripatetic philosophy, but the author is critiquing it.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the logicians' definition in the passage, what is the 'special characteristic' (khassa)?
A) An accident that is necessary but not constant for all individuals of a single universal
B) An accident that is a necessary and constant attribute of all individuals of a single universal alone
C) An accident that is separable and not necessary
D) Any attribute that is not a genus, species, differentia, or special characteristic

Key Takeaway
Khassa is a constant accident of all individuals of one universal only; 'arad 'amm is not exclusive.

Critique of Two Definitions of 'Arad
Divides 'aradi into khassa and 'arad 'amm and critiques two definitions of 'arad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate two faulty definitions of 'arad based on the author's critiques.

Core Idea
Defining 'arad by negation of other universals or by perishability fails to cover all instances.

The author presents two common definitions of accident and demonstrates their shortcomings. This lesson analyzes the logical flaws in these definitions, emphasizing the need for comprehensive and precise definitions in logic.

Guided Path
1
State the first definition
The first definition says: 'Accident is what is not a genus, not a species, not a differentia, not a special characteristic, subsisting in the subject.' This is a negative definition.
2
Identify the first problem
The author points out that 'subsisting in the subject' does not guarantee it is an accident, because some substances also exist in a subject (contradiction). Also, defining by negation is weak because it lacks priority.
The author suggests that if negation is used, one could simply say 'it is not the other four universals' due to exhaustiveness.
3
State the second definition
The second definition says: 'Accident is what perishes without the corruption of its bearer.' That is, it is separable.
4
Identify the second problem
The author shows that constant accidents like the blackness of a crow do not perish unless their bearer perishes, so they are not covered by this definition.
The author notes that if considered with respect to imagination (wahm), the definition could be valid, but that is far from apparent meaning and against the craft of logicians.

وأعلم أن ما أشرنا إلى تحقيقه من قسمي العرض إنما هو على ما اقتضته الخاصة وإلا فالمشهور عن الأوائل أنهم كانوا يقسمون العرض إلى ما هو لازم دائم لكلية كلي واحد دون غيره، وإلى ما ليس كذلك، فما كان من الأول خصوه باسم الخاصة، وما كان من الثاني أبقوه على إطلاقه وسموه (عرضاً) وسواء كان ذلك خاصاً بكلي واحد أو أكثر، وسواء كان دائماً أو مفارقاً كما سبق تعريفه، حتى إنهم كانوا يدرجون في ذلك كتابة الإنسان وضحكه بالفعل، ولا حرج في الاصطلاح بعد فهم المعنى. وإن تشوّقت إلى رسم الخاصة والعرض على مصطلحهم، فالخاصة ما هو عارض لجميع جزئيات كلي واحد وحده دائماً، وهو كما أشرنا إليه الخاصية المنطقية سابقاً. الفصل الثامن: في قسمة العرضي إلى الخاصة والعرض العام وأما العرض فقد قيل فيه ما ليس بجنس ولا نوع ولا فصل ولا خاصة، قائم في الموضوع. وقيل هو ما يكون يفسد من غير فساد حامله وفي ما بين العبارتين تقصر، أما الأولى فلأنا قد بينا أن العرض ليس هو قسيم الجوهر، وهو ما وجوده في موضوع، بل قد يكون العرضي جوهراً كما سبق تحقيقه، وإذ ذاك فلا يكون الرسم جامعاً. كيف وأن تعريف الشيء بسلب حقيقة غيره عنه بعيد لعدم الأولوية، وأن قدر أن سلب ما أخذ في التعريف أظهر كان الاقتصار عليه كافياً، وهو أن يقال هو ما ليس بجنس ولا نوع ولا فصل ولا خاصة ضرورة الانحصار، وأن ما أخذ في التعريف أظهر. وأما العبارة الثالثة فإنها أيضاً غير جامعة إذ العارض منه ما هو دائم في الوجود غير مفارق كسواد الغراب والفطوسة للأنف ونحوه، ولا يفسد إلا وقد فسد حامله وإلا لكان مفارقاً نعم، لو أخذ ذلك بالنظر إلى الوهم كان صحيحاً، فإنه لا يلزم فساد الغراب عند توهم كونه غير أسود، فإنه من حيث هو هو ليس مما يتوقف على كونه أسود أو أبيض أو غير ذلك، لكنه مع ذلك بعيد عن ظاهر اللفظ ومتوقف في فهمه على أمر زائد، وذلك مما تأباه صناعة المنطقيين في الحدود والرسوم.
And know that what we indicated regarding the verification of the two divisions of accident is only according to what the special characteristic required. Otherwise, the famous view of the ancients is that they used to divide accident into what is a necessary constant attribute for every single universal exclusively, and into what is not like that. What was of the first they named 'special characteristic', and what was of the second they left it unrestricted and called it 'accident', whether that was specific to one universal or more, and whether constant or separable, as its definition preceded. They even included in that the writing of man and his actual laughter, and there is no harm in terminology after understanding the meaning. And if you desire a definition of the special characteristic and accident according to their terminology, the special characteristic is what is an accident of all individuals of a single universal alone constantly, and it is, as we indicated, the logical property earlier. Chapter Eight: On the Division of Accident into Special Characteristic and General Accident. As for accident, it has been said that it is what is not a genus, nor a species, nor a differentia, nor a special characteristic, subsisting in the subject. And it has been said that it is what perishes without the corruption of its bearer. Between the two expressions there is a shortcoming. As for the first, we have shown that accident is not the correlative of substance, which is what exists in a subject; rather, the accident could be a substance as verified previously, and then the definition is not comprehensive. Moreover, defining a thing by negating the reality of something else from it is far because of lack of priority, and if it is assumed that the negation taken in the definition is more obvious, it would be sufficient to limit oneself to it, and that is to say: it is what is not a genus, nor a species, nor a differentia, nor a special characteristic, due to the necessity of enumeration, and what is taken in the definition is more obvious. As for the third expression, it is also not comprehensive, because from the accident is what is constant in existence, non-separable, like the blackness of the crow and the cat-like nose and the like, and it does not perish except when its bearer perishes, otherwise it would be separable. Yes, if that is taken with consideration to the imagination, it is correct, because it is not necessary that the crow perish when imagining it being non-black, for in itself it is not dependent on being black or white or other than that. However, with that it is far from the apparent meaning and dependent in its understanding on an additional matter, and that is repugnant to the craft of logicians in definitions and descriptions.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage begins by clarifying a point about the division of accident ('arad). The author states that the earlier discussion (possibly referring to a previous section) about the two types of accident was according to the concept of 'special characteristic' (khassa). However, the famous view of the ancient logicians is different: they divide accident into that which is a necessary and constant attribute of a single universal exclusively (i.e., the property that belongs to all individuals of that universal and only them) and into that which is not like that. The first they named 'special characteristic' (khassa), and the second they left with the general name 'accident' ('arad), regardless of whether it is specific to one universal or more, and whether it is constant (like the blackness of a crow) or separable (like writing). They even include examples like man's writing and actual laughter. The author then provides the definition of khassa according to their terminology: it is what is an accident of all individuals of a single universal alone constantly. This is the logical property (khassiya) mentioned earlier.

The passage then continues with the chapter heading 'On the Division of Accident into Special Characteristic and General Accident'. Here, the author presents two definitions of accident ('arad) that were commonly given. The first definition is negative: 'accident is what is not a genus, not a species, not a differentia, and not a special characteristic, subsisting in the subject.' The second definition is: 'it is what perishes without the corruption of its bearer.' The author critiques both.

Regarding the first definition, the author points out two problems. First, accident is not simply the correlative of substance (jawhar) in the sense of 'what exists in a subject', because sometimes an accident can be a substance (as previously verified). Thus the definition is not comprehensive (jami'). Second, defining something by negating the reality of something else is problematic due to lack of priority. If the negation is more obvious, it would suffice to simply say 'it is not genus, not species, not differentia, not special characteristic' due to the necessary enumeration (implies that these five universals exhaust the categories, so the negation serves as a definition by exhaustion). But this is still not a proper definition.

As for the third expression (the second definition), the author says it is also not comprehensive. Why? Because some accidents are constant in existence and non-separable, such as the blackness of a crow or the cat-like nose of a certain nose shape. These do not perish unless their bearer perishes. So the definition 'perishes without corruption of bearer' does not cover constant accidents. The author notes that if one considers this definition with respect to the imagination (wahm), it could be valid: imagining the crow not black does not require the crow to perish. However, this is far from the apparent meaning of the definition and requires additional understanding, which is against the craft of logicians in definitions and descriptions.

In summary, the passage teaches that the division of 'aradi into khassa and 'arad 'amm is based on necessity and exclusivity, and it critiques two faulty definitions of 'arad, showing why they fail to capture all instances.

Key Terms
خاصة (khāṣṣa): A special characteristic (property) that is a necessary and constant accident of all individuals of a single universal exclusively. [Note: In some texts, khassa is used interchangeably with 'property' as one of the five universals; here it is specifically the 'logical property' as per the logicians' terminology.]
عرض عام (ʿaraḍ ʿāmm): A general accident that is not necessarily exclusive to a single universal; it can be present in multiple universals, and it can be either constant or separable.
عرض (ʿaraḍ): In the context of the five universals, it is the accident as a category; here it is the subject of division into khassa and 'arad 'amm. The passage critiques two definitions: one by negation of other universals, and one by perishability without bearer. [Note: In earlier Aristotelian logic, 'arad is often defined as 'what exists in a subject', but here the author shows that this definition conflates with substance.]
قيام في الموضوع (qiyām fī al-mawḍūʿ): Subsisting in the subject, i.e., existing as an accident in a substance. The author notes that this is insufficient because some 'aradi are substances. [Note: This is a standard definition of accident in Peripatetic philosophy, but the author is critiquing it.]

Key Takeaway
A proper definition must be inclusive of all instances and exclusive of non-instances; definitions by negation or by perishability fail these criteria.

Critiquing Definitions of 'Arad (Accidental Universal)
Critique of three definitions of 'arad (accident).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the three common definitions of 'arad using the criteria of comprehensiveness and clarity as per the author's critique.

Core Idea
Defining by negation alone is logically valid but rhetorically weak.

In this passage, the author critically examines three definitions of 'arad (the accidental universal). He shows that the first definition, which excludes genus, species, differentia, and special characteristic, is logically valid but depends on exhaustive division. The second definition, based on inseparability, fails for permanent accidents. The third, relying on mental estimation, is correct but departs from literal meaning. Understanding these critiques sharpens your ability to evaluate definitions in logic and Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three definitions
Locate in the Arabic the three formulations: (1) 'what is not a genus, species, differentia, or special characteristic, subsisting in a subject'; (2) 'that which ceases to exist without the corruption of its substrate'; (3) the definition based on estimation (al-wahm) where the accident is imagined absent without affecting the substrate.
The first is negative; the second is based on existential dependence; the third is counterfactual.
2
Analyze the critique of the first definition
The author objects that 'aradi can sometimes be a substance (as previously argued), so the definition that excludes substance is not comprehensive. Also, defining by negating others is remote and lacks priority.
The first definition fails the comprehensiveness criterion.
3
Analyze the critique of the second and third definitions
The second definition fails because some accidents (e.g., blackness of a crow) are permanent and only cease when the substrate perishes. The third definition is correct in terms of mental estimation but is not literal and requires extra explanation, which logicians dislike.
The author uses counterexamples (crow's blackness) and rhetorical norms.
4
Reconstruct the author's preferred definition
The author suggests that the only valid definition is the negative one restricted to 'not a genus, species, differentia, or special characteristic' because it follows from exhaustive division. He admits it is not ideal but is logically sound.
The author prioritizes logical completeness over rhetorical convenience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثامن: في قسمة العرضي إلى الخاصة والعرض العام وهو أبداً وأما العرض فقد قيل فيه ما ليس بجنس ولا نوع ولا فصل ولا خاصة، قائم في الموضوع. وقيل هو ما يكون يفسد من غير فساد حامله وفي ما بين العبارتين ،تقصر ، أما الأولى فلأنا قد بينا أن العرض ليس هو قسيم الجوهر، وهو ما وجوده في موضوع، بل قد يكون العرضي جوهراً كما سبق تحقيقه، وإذ ذاك فلا يكون الرسم جامعاً. كيف وأن تعريف الشيء بسلب حقيقة غيره عنه بعيد لعدم الأولوية، وأن قدر أن سلب ما أخذ في التعريف أظهر كان الاقتصار عليه كافياً، وهو أن يقال هو ما ليس بجنس ولا نوع ولا فصل ولا خاصة ضرورة الانحصار، وأن ما أخذ في التعريف أظهر. وأما العبارة الثالثة فإنها أيضاً غير جامعة إذ العارض منه ما هو دائم في الوجود غیر مفارق کـ (سواد الغراب والفطوسة للأنف ونحوه، ولا يفسد إلا وقد فسد حامله وإلا لكان مفارقاً نعم، لو أخذ ذلك بالنظر إلى الوهم كان صحيحاً، فإنه لا يلزم فساد الغراب عند توهم كونه غير أسود، فإنه من حيث هو هو ليس مما يتوقف على كونه أسود أو أبيض أو غير ذلك، لكنه مع ذلك بعيد عن ظاهر اللفظ ومتوقف في فهمه على أمر زائد، وذلك مما تأباه صناعة المنطقيين في الحدود والرسوم.
Chapter Eight: On the division of the accidental universal (al-'aradi) into the special characteristic (al-khassa) and the common accident (al-'arad al-'amm). And it is eternally? As for 'arad (accident), it has been said: 'It is what is not a genus, not a species, not a differentia, and not a special characteristic, subsisting in a subject.' And it was said: 'It is that which ceases to exist without the corruption of its substrate.' And between the two expressions there is shortcoming. As for the first: because we have explained that 'arad is not the correlative of substance, and it is what has its existence in a subject; rather, al-'aradi may be a substance as previously established; if so, then the description (rasm) is not comprehensive. Moreover, defining a thing by negating the essence of something else is remote due to lack of priority. If it is assumed that the negation taken in the definition is more evident, then restricting it to that would be sufficient, i.e., to say: 'It is what is not a genus, not a species, not a differentia, and not a special characteristic' due to necessity of exhaustive division, and what is taken in the definition is more evident. As for the third expression, it also is not comprehensive, because the accidental (al-'arid) includes what is permanent in existence, inseparable, like the blackness of the crow and the flatness of the nose and the like; it does not cease except when its substrate ceases, otherwise it would be separable. Yes, if one takes that with reference to estimation (al-wahm), it would be correct, for it is not necessary that the crow cease to exist when it is estimated as being non-black; for it, considered in itself, does not depend on being black or white or otherwise. But despite that, it is remote from the apparent meaning of the word and requires something extra for its understanding, which the craft of logicians abhors in definitions and descriptions.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage critiques three definitions of 'arad (accident). The first definition, that 'arad is what is not genus, species, differentia, or special characteristic and subsists in a subject, is rejected because the accidental universal ('aradi) can sometimes be a substance, as clarified earlier. Thus the definition fails to be comprehensive. The second definition, that 'arad is what ceases to exist without the corruption of its substrate, is also insufficient because some accidents are permanent and inseparable, like the blackness of a crow; they only cease when the substrate itself perishes. The third definition, which relies on mental estimation (al-wahm) — i.e., the accident can be imagined absent without considering the substrate's corruption — is logically correct but practically undesirable, as it departs from the literal meaning and requires additional explanation. The author concludes that the only valid definition is the negative one (not genus, species, etc.) based on exhaustive division, though it is not ideal. The passage thus emphasizes that definitions of logical terms must be precise, comprehensive, and clear.

العرض العام
الخاصة

Key Takeaway
A sound definition must be comprehensive and clear; mere negation suffices logically but is rhetorically deficient.

The Famous Division of the Five Universals
Author presents the famous division of the five universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reproduce the famous division of the five universals by tracing the two binary splits: essential vs accidental, and answer to 'what is it?' vs 'which one?'.

Core Idea
The five universals derive from two binary distinctions: essential vs accidental and 'what is it?' vs 'which one?'.

In this passage, the author lays out the famous division of the five universals (al-kulliyāt al-khams). This division is a classic tool for classifying general terms. We will walk through the dichotomous splits to build the classification and then see its limitations in the next lesson.

Guided Path
1
Identify the starting point
The division begins with a single term that denotes many things (al-lafẓ al-mufrad al-dāll ʿalā al-kathīrīn). This is the absolute universal.
2
First split: species difference vs number difference
Check whether the denoted things differ in species (mukhtalifān bi-al-nawʿ) or only in number (bi-al-ʿadad). This is the root dichotomy.
If they differ in species, proceed to step 3; if only in number, skip to step 4.
3
Species-different branch: essential vs accidental
For terms denoting things differing in species, determine if the term is essential (dhātī) or accidental (ʿaraḍī). If essential, go to step 4; if accidental, it is the general accident (al-ʿaraḍ al-ʿāmm).
4
Essential branch: 'what is it?' vs 'which one?'
Essential terms are further divided by the question they answer. If the term is said in answer to 'what is it?' (mā), it is the genus (jins). If in answer to 'which one?' (ayy), it is the differentia (faṣl).
5
Number-different branch: 'what is it?' vs 'which one?'
For terms denoting things differing only in number, apply the same question distinction. If said in answer to 'what is it?', it is the species (nawʿ). If in answer to 'which one?', it is the property (khāṣṣa).
This completes the famous five.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل التاسع في حصر الكليات الخمس إلى الجنس والنوع والفصل والخاصة والعرض العام ووجه انقسام الكلي المطلق إليها وتتبع ما قيل فيه والقسمة المشهورة لذلك، أن اللفظ المفرد الدال على الكثيرين إما مختلفان بالنوع أو العدد، فإن كان الأول فإما أن يكون ذاتياً، وإما أن يكون عرضياً، فإن كان عرضياً فهو العرض العام، وإن كان ذاتياً، فإما أن يقال في جواب ما، وإما في جواب أي، فإن كان في جواب ما فهو الجنس، وإن كان في جواب أي فهو الفصل، وإن كان الثاني، فإما أن يكون مقولاً في جواب ما أو أي فإن كان مقولاً في جواب ما فهو النوع، وإن كان مقولاً في جواب أي فهو الخاصة. ولا يخفى أن طبيعة الفصل بما هو فصل خارجة عن هذه القسمة، فإنها لا تتناول من الفصول إلا ما كان من فصول الأجناس دون فصول أنواع الأنواع؛ إذ هي غير مقولة على مختلفين بالحقيقة، بل وطبيعة الخاصة بما هي خاصة أيضاً شاذة عنها لعدم دخول خواص الأجناس فيها وإن لم تشذ، وليس كل ما يقال على نوع واحد أو أشياء لا تختلف إلا بالعدد فلا تكون مقولاً في جواب ما فهو خاصة، بل يجوز أن تكون فصلاً وذلك مما لم نتعرض القسمة للفرق فيه، ومع هذا كله فالنوع بالمعنى المضاف غير داخل فيها، وإن قطعنا النظر عنه فالقسمة لما سواه من الكليات ليس إلا ما أشرنا إليه أولاً من بيان انقسام الكلي الحاصلة الذاتي والعرضي، وانقسام الذاتي إلى الجنس والنوع والفصل وانقسام العرضي إلى الخاصة والعرض العام، وقد عرف وجه الحقيقة في ما سبق
Chapter Nine: On enumerating the five universals: genus, species, differentia, property, and general accident, and the manner of division of the absolute universal into them, and a review of what has been said about it. The famous division for that is: the single term denoting many things is either differing in species or in number. If the first [differing in species], then it is either essential or accidental. If accidental, it is the general accident. If essential, then either said in answer to 'what is it?' or in answer to 'which one?'. If said in answer to 'what is it?', it is the genus; if in answer to 'which one?', it is the differentia. If the second [differing in number], then it is either said in answer to 'what is it?' or 'which one?'. If said in answer to 'what is it?', it is the species; if said in answer to 'which one?', it is the property. It is not hidden that the nature of differentia as such is outside this division, for it only includes among differentiate those that are differentiate of genera, not differentiate of species of species, since it is not predicated of things differing in reality. Rather, the nature of property as such is also irregular with respect to it, because properties of genera are not included in it, even if they are not irregular. And not everything that is said of a single species or things that do not differ except in number and is not said in answer to 'what is it?' is a property; rather, it may be a differentia, and the division has not addressed the distinction. With all this, the species in the relative sense is not included in it. And if we disregard it, the division for what remains of the universals is only what we first indicated: the division of the universal into essential and accidental, and the division of the essential into genus, species, and differentia, and the division of the accidental into property and general accident. And the true aspect has been known in what preceded.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage defines the famous division of the five universals (al-kulliyāt al-khams) and then critically examines its limitations. The author begins by stating the division: a single term denoting many things (al-lafẓ al-mufrad al-dāll ʿalā al-kathīrīn) is either differing in species (mukhtalifān bi-al-nawʿ) or in number (bi-al-ʿadad). If differing in species, it is either essential (dhātī) or accidental (ʿaraḍī). Accidental gives the general accident (al-ʿaraḍ al-ʿāmm). Essential is divided by whether it is said in answer to 'what is it?' (mā) or 'which one?' (ayy). If in answer to 'what is it?', it is genus (jins); if 'which one?', it is differentia (faṣl). If differing only in number, it is either said in answer to 'what is it?' (species, nawʿ) or 'which one?' (property, khāṣṣa). The author then notes several problems: First, the nature of differentia as such (ṭabīʿat al-faṣl bimā huwa faṣl) falls outside this division because it only covers differentiate of genera, not differentiate of species of species (fuṣūl anwāʿ al-anwāʿ), since the latter are not predicated of things differing in reality. Second, property as such also has an irregularity: properties of genera are not included. Third, not everything said of a single species that is not said in answer to 'what is it?' is necessarily a property; it could be a differentia. Fourth, the relative species (al-nawʿ bi-al-maʿnā al-muḍāf) is not included. The author then states that if we set aside the relative species, the correct exhaustive division is: the universal is divided into essential and accidental; essential into genus, species, and differentia; accidental into property and general accident. This encapsulates the true nature of the division.

Key Terms

Shortcomings of the Famous Division
Author critiques the famous division and proposes a corrective.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify four limitations of the famous division as pointed out by the author and explain how he resolves them.

Core Idea
The famous division fails to capture the nature of differentia as such, properties of genera, and the relative species.

After presenting the famous division, the author critically examines it and notes several gaps. This lesson explores those shortcomings and the author's proposed corrective. Understanding the limitations of a classification is as important as knowing the classification itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first problem: nature of differentia
The division only includes differentiate that are differentiate of genera (fuṣūl al-ajnās). It excludes differentiate of species of species (fuṣūl anwāʿ al-anwāʿ), because the latter are not predicated of things differing in reality. For example, 'sensitive' differentiates animal from plant, but 'sensitive' is itself a genus relative to 'rational'.
The nature of differentia as such (ṭabīʿat al-faṣl bimā huwa faṣl) falls outside the division.
2
Identify the second problem: properties of genera
The division also excludes properties of genera (khawāṣṣ al-ajnās). For instance, 'moving' is a property of animal, but it applies to all animals, not just one species. The division only includes properties of species.
The author notes that properties of genera are 'irregular' (shādhdha) in this classification.
3
Identify the third problem: ambiguity between property and differentia
Not everything said of a single species that does not answer 'what is it?' is necessarily a property; it could be a differentia. The division does not distinguish between them, leaving a loophole.
Example: 'risible' (laughing) is a property of humans, but 'rational' is a differentia. Both answer 'which one?'.
4
Identify the fourth problem: relative species excluded
The relative species (al-nawʿ bi-al-maʿnā al-muḍāf) – a species considered relative to a higher genus – is not included in the division. For example, 'animal' is a genus relative to 'human' but a species relative to 'body'.
5
Examine the author's corrective division
The author states that if we disregard the relative species, the exhaustive division is: universal → essential (genus, species, differentia) and accidental (property, general accident). This simpler division avoids the gaps of the famous one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل التاسع في حصر الكليات الخمس إلى الجنس والنوع والفصل والخاصة والعرض العام ووجه انقسام الكلي المطلق إليها وتتبع ما قيل فيه والقسمة المشهورة لذلك، أن اللفظ المفرد الدال على الكثيرين إما مختلفان بالنوع أو العدد، فإن كان الأول فإما أن يكون ذاتياً، وإما أن يكون عرضياً، فإن كان عرضياً فهو العرض العام، وإن كان ذاتياً، فإما أن يقال في جواب ما، وإما في جواب أي، فإن كان في جواب ما فهو الجنس، وإن كان في جواب أي فهو الفصل، وإن كان الثاني، فإما أن يكون مقولاً في جواب ما أو أي فإن كان مقولاً في جواب ما فهو النوع، وإن كان مقولاً في جواب أي فهو الخاصة. ولا يخفى أن طبيعة الفصل بما هو فصل خارجة عن هذه القسمة، فإنها لا تتناول من الفصول إلا ما كان من فصول الأجناس دون فصول أنواع الأنواع؛ إذ هي غير مقولة على مختلفين بالحقيقة، بل وطبيعة الخاصة بما هي خاصة أيضاً شاذة عنها لعدم دخول خواص الأجناس فيها وإن لم تشذ، وليس كل ما يقال على نوع واحد أو أشياء لا تختلف إلا بالعدد فلا تكون مقولاً في جواب ما فهو خاصة، بل يجوز أن تكون فصلاً وذلك مما لم نتعرض القسمة للفرق فيه، ومع هذا كله فالنوع بالمعنى المضاف غير داخل فيها، وإن قطعنا النظر عنه فالقسمة لما سواه من الكليات ليس إلا ما أشرنا إليه أولاً من بيان انقسام الكلي الحاصلة الذاتي والعرضي، وانقسام الذاتي إلى الجنس والنوع والفصل وانقسام العرضي إلى الخاصة والعرض العام، وقد عرف وجه الحقيقة في ما سبق
Chapter Nine: On enumerating the five universals: genus, species, differentia, property, and general accident, and the manner of division of the absolute universal into them, and a review of what has been said about it. The famous division for that is: the single term denoting many things is either differing in species or in number. If the first [differing in species], then it is either essential or accidental. If accidental, it is the general accident. If essential, then either said in answer to 'what is it?' or in answer to 'which one?'. If said in answer to 'what is it?', it is the genus; if in answer to 'which one?', it is the differentia. If the second [differing in number], then it is either said in answer to 'what is it?' or 'which one?'. If said in answer to 'what is it?', it is the species; if said in answer to 'which one?', it is the property. It is not hidden that the nature of differentia as such is outside this division, for it only includes among differentiate those that are differentiate of genera, not differentiate of species of species, since it is not predicated of things differing in reality. Rather, the nature of property as such is also irregular with respect to it, because properties of genera are not included in it, even if they are not irregular. And not everything that is said of a single species or things that do not differ except in number and is not said in answer to 'what is it?' is a property; rather, it may be a differentia, and the division has not addressed the distinction. With all this, the species in the relative sense is not included in it. And if we disregard it, the division for what remains of the universals is only what we first indicated: the division of the universal into essential and accidental, and the division of the essential into genus, species, and differentia, and the division of the accidental into property and general accident. And the true aspect has been known in what preceded.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage defines the famous division of the five universals (al-kulliyāt al-khams) and then critically examines its limitations. The author begins by stating the division: a single term denoting many things (al-lafẓ al-mufrad al-dāll ʿalā al-kathīrīn) is either differing in species (mukhtalifān bi-al-nawʿ) or in number (bi-al-ʿadad). If differing in species, it is either essential (dhātī) or accidental (ʿaraḍī). Accidental gives the general accident (al-ʿaraḍ al-ʿāmm). Essential is divided by whether it is said in answer to 'what is it?' (mā) or 'which one?' (ayy). If in answer to 'what is it?', it is genus (jins); if 'which one?', it is differentia (faṣl). If differing only in number, it is either said in answer to 'what is it?' (species, nawʿ) or 'which one?' (property, khāṣṣa). The author then notes several problems: First, the nature of differentia as such (ṭabīʿat al-faṣl bimā huwa faṣl) falls outside this division because it only covers differentiate of genera, not differentiate of species of species (fuṣūl anwāʿ al-anwāʿ), since the latter are not predicated of things differing in reality. Second, property as such also has an irregularity: properties of genera are not included. Third, not everything said of a single species that is not said in answer to 'what is it?' is necessarily a property; it could be a differentia. Fourth, the relative species (al-nawʿ bi-al-maʿnā al-muḍāf) is not included. The author then states that if we set aside the relative species, the correct exhaustive division is: the universal is divided into essential and accidental; essential into genus, species, and differentia; accidental into property and general accident. This encapsulates the true nature of the division.

خواص الأجناس

Key Takeaway
The famous division is not exhaustive; the author's corrective – dividing the universal into essential and accidental – avoids the gaps.

Critiquing Divisions of the Five Universals
Author critiques alternative divisions of the five universals and proposes a refined division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate alternative divisions of the five universals by checking for exhaustiveness and avoiding circularity.

Core Idea
A valid division of universals must be exhaustive and avoid circularity; the relative species complicates simple dichotomies.

This lesson examines attempts to divide the five universals, particularly to include the relative species (al-nawʿ al-muḍāf). The author critiques two earlier divisions, pointing out logical flaws such as non-exhaustiveness and circularity. Understanding these critiques sharpens our ability to evaluate any classification system in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two divisions presented
First division: the single term is essential or accidental; if essential, it is predicated of things differing in number or in reality, etc. Second division (by the later scholar): universal term is essential or accidental; if essential, it is either said in answer to 'what is it?' or not; if said, then more specific or more general.
The second division introduces 'more specific' and 'more general'.
2
Extract the author's critique of the first division
The author points out that the nature of genus as such (ṭabīʿat al-jins) is not included because it only covers high genera, not intermediate or low. Similarly, the relative species as relative is excluded because it only covers high or intermediate relative species.
This shows the division fails to cover all cases.
3
Analyze the critique of the later scholar's division
The division into more specific and more general is not exhaustive because a term can be neither more general nor more specific (e.g., coextensive terms unless intended). Also, the further division of relative species into fit and not fit to be genus leads to an extra division that breaks the exclusivity.
The author uses the condition of exhaustiveness (qisma ḥāṣira).
4
Assess the author's proposed division
The author begins a better division: universal term is essential or non-essential; if essential, either said in answer to 'what is it?' or not; if said, then considered from aspect of being more general or more specific. The passage cuts off, but the direction is to use a single criterion (answer to 'what is it?') and then apply a relative generality.
This aims to be exhaustive and avoid circularity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أردنا إدراج النوع بالمعنى المضاف في القسمة بقسمة أخرى فليس على نحو ما قيل إن اللفظ المفرد الدال إما ذاتي وإما عرضي، فإن كان عرضياً فمنه الخاصة والعرض العام على ما عرف وإن كان ذاتياً فإما أن يقال على مختلفين بالعدد أو الحقيقة، فإن كان الأول فهو النوع بالمعنى الأول، وإن كان الثاني فإما أن يكون مقولاً ولا يقال عليه أو يقال عليه، فإن كان الأول فهو الجنس، وإن كان الثاني فهو النوع بالمعنى المضاف. وإنه لا يخفى أيضاً أن طبيعة الجنس بما هو جنس غير داخلة في القسمة؛ إذ هي غير متناولة إلا لما كان من الأجناس عالياً دون المتوسط والسافل، وكذا طبيعة النوع المضاف بما هو مضاف أيضاً خارجة عنها، ولا تناول لها منه، إلا ما كان منه عالياً أو متوسطاً دون سافل. ومما يلحق بهذه القسمة في الفساد أيضاً ما أومأ إليه أفضل المتأخرين وهو قوله اللفظ المفرد الكلي إما أن يكون ذاتياً وإما عرضياً، فإن كان ذاتياً فإما أن يقال في جواب ما أو ليس فإن لم يقل في جواب ما فهو الفصل، وإن قيل فمنه ما هو أخص ومنه ما هو أعمّ، فالأعمّ هو الجنس، والأخص هو النوع بالمعنى المضاف ثم النوع بالمعنى المضاف منه ما لا يصلح أن يكون جنساً ومنه ما يصلح، فما لا يصلح هو النوع بالمعنى الآخر، وأما العرضي فقسمته إلى الخاصة والعرض العام، فعلى نحو ما سبق وهو غير سديد؛ فإنه وإن كان من الذاتي ما هو أخص وأعم فليست القسمة فيها حاصرة، لجواز أن لا يكون أعم ولا أخص. اللهم إلا أن يريد بالأخص المساوي وهو بعيد عن ظاهر اللفظ، وإن قدر الانحصار في القسمين، فلا يخفى أن قسمة النوع بالمعنى المضاف إلى ما هو صالح أن يكون جنساً وإلى ما ليس وإن اندرج فيه النوع بالمعنى الآخـر غـيـر المضاف، غير أنه يوجب قسماً آخر وهو ما وقع قسيماً للنوع بالمعنى غير المضاف تحت النوع بالمعنى المضاف وفيه إبطال الحصر. فالأقرب في ذلك أن يقال اللفظ الكلي إما ذاتي وإما غير ذاتي، فإن كان ذاتياً فإما أن يقال في جواب ما أو ليس فما لا يقال في جواب ما هو الفصل وما يقال في جواب ما، فإما أن ينظر إليه من جهة أنه أعم مقولين في جواب ما أو أخص
And if we want to include the species in the relative sense in the division with another division, it is not as it was said: the single denoting term is either essential or accidental. If accidental, then from it are property and general accident as known. If essential, then it is either said of things differing in number or in reality. If the first, it is the species in the first sense. If the second, then either it is predicated and nothing is predicated of it, or something is predicated of it. If the first, it is the genus; if the second, it is the species in the relative sense. It is also not hidden that the nature of genus as such is not included in the division, for it only includes what is of the genera that are high, not the intermediate and low. Likewise, the nature of the relative species as relative is also outside it; it only includes from it what is high or intermediate, not low. And among what joins this division in corruption is also what was pointed to by the most excellent of the later scholars: his statement: the single universal term is either essential or accidental. If essential, then it is either said in answer to 'what is it?' or not. If not said in answer to 'what is it?', it is the differentia. If it is said, then from it there is what is more specific and what is more general. The more general is the genus, the more specific is the species in the relative sense. Then the relative species includes what is not fit to be a genus and what is fit. What is not fit is the species in the other sense. As for the accidental, its division into property and general accident is as previously, and it is not sound. For even if among the essential there is what is more specific and more general, the division is not exhaustive, because it is possible that it is neither more general nor more specific. Unless he means by 'more specific' the coextensive (musāwī), which is far from the apparent meaning of the wording. And if the exclusivity to the two divisions is assumed, it is not hidden that the division of the relative species into what is fit to be a genus and what is not, although it includes the species in the other non-relative sense, entails another division, namely what becomes a counterpart of the non-relative species under the relative species, and this invalidates the exclusivity. Therefore, the closest in that is to say: the universal term is either essential or non-essential. If essential, then it is either said in answer to 'what is it?' or not. What is not said in answer to 'what is it?' is the differentia. As for what is said in answer to 'what is it?', it is either considered from the aspect that it is more general among things said in answer to 'what is it?' or more specific.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage discusses alternative ways to divide the five universals, particularly to include the relative species (al-nawʿ al-muḍāf). The author first presents a division that tries to include the relative species: the single term is essential or accidental; if essential, it is predicated of things differing in number (first species, i.e., the species in the absolute sense) or in reality; if the latter, then either nothing is predicated of it (genus) or something is predicated of it (relative species). The author immediately points out that this division fails to include the nature of genus as such (ṭabīʿat al-jins), because it only covers high genera, not intermediate or low. Similarly, the relative species as relative is also excluded because it only covers high or intermediate relative species, not low. Then the author critiques the division proposed by the 'most excellent of the later scholars' (afḍal al-muta'akhkhirīn). That scholar's division: universal term is essential or accidental; essential is either said in answer to 'what is it?' or not; if not, it is differentia; if said, then among what is said, some is more specific and some more general; the more general is genus, the more specific is relative species; then relative species is further divided into what is fit to be a genus and what is not, the latter being the other species (the non-relative species). The author finds this unsound because the initial division into more specific and more general is not exhaustive; it is possible that the term is neither more general nor more specific. Unless by 'more specific' the scholar means 'coextensive' (musāwī), but that is far from the apparent meaning. Even if we assume exclusivity, the further division of relative species into fit and not fit leads to another division that invalidates the exclusivity. Finally, the author proposes what is closest to correctness: the universal term is either essential or non-essential; if essential, it is either said in answer to 'what is it?' or not; if not, it is differentia; if said, then it is considered from the aspect of being more general or more specific among things said in answer to 'what is it?'. The passage ends abruptly with this proposal.
